Subject: [PLUG] Memtest86 errors

One of my computers has been crashing unexpectedly on
occasion. I ran Memtest86, and there are some errors,
though they did not show up on the first pass.

Removal of RAM is the traditional way to correct such
errors. However, I understand that when all such
errors occur within a rather small range of addresses,
as in this case, Linux can be instructed not to use
these addresses. Can anyone refer me to sources of
information on this?

From johnxj at comcast.net  Sat Jul  2 12:40:26 2005
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jordan)
Date: Sat, 2 Jul 2005 12:40:26 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] iPod with Linux?
Message-ID: <42C68B3A.21290.94FC685@localhost>

OK, I scheduled this weekend for some free time to see if I could 
get my shiny new iPod working with my Ubuntu 5.04 computer. 
After googling for hours and installing grip and gtkpod, I've made 
some progress, but remain confused about most of it.

I managed to rip a Stravinsky CD from my collection. Grip did a 
nice job -- there are 25 tracks in .ogg format in 
/home/jjj/mp3/stravinsky_igor/firebird. If I right-click on any of them I 
am presented with the option to play them with Music Player 
(Ubuntu default) or Realplayer. They play fine through the computer 
with Music Player, but I have not gotten Realplayer installed 
properly. (I think it is a 32-bit vs 64-bit issue, but I'll deal with that 
later.)

Where I am falling down is getting the tracks into the iPod. When I 
plug it into a USB port Ubuntu pops up a folder window and 
appears to be able to read the filesystem. (This iPod has never 
been plugged into a Windows or Mac computer, and has never 
been near iTunes, which I have never installed on anything.)

Evidently gtkpod is the application that is supposed to transfer the 
tracks to the iPod. But I'll be danged if I can get it to do so. 
Clicking on Help does nothing because the person who wrote 
gtkpod evidently never got around to creating a Help document, nor 
does Google find any instructions. Looking at the gtkpod window, 
nothing is obvious. There is a button labeled "Dirs" and when I click 
on it I can browse to the folder where it sees the ogg files. I select 
one and click on "Add recursively" (whatever "recursively" means) 
and it says "processing" for a moment, then nothing. The ogg file 
does not appear in the gtkpod window. 

I also tried just dragging one of the ogg files to a folder in the iPod 
window. I'm pretty sure that is not the way to do it, because from 
my reading there is a database file on the iPod that must be 
updated. But I tried it anyway, just for kicks. Ubuntu popped up a 
box that said I did not have permission to write to the iPod.

I think I'm missing something that is so basic that no one ever 
wrote it down in any of the wikis and how-tos I have read. Like 
"before you can use the computer you must turn it on." OK, maybe 
not quite that basic. :(

Part of my confusion is that I have never used an iPod on a 
Windows or Mac, so I don't know how it's supposed to work in the 
first place. And the little instruction manual that Apple included 
with the iPod just refers to iTunes. 

This would probably be easier if I were a teenager. :)

Does anyone have any idea what I might be missing here?



From ckonstan at lunarlogic.com  Sat Jul  2 12:59:19 2005
From: ckonstan at lunarlogic.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Sat, 2 Jul 2005 12:59:19 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] iPod with Linux?
In-Reply-To: <42C68B3A.21290.94FC685@localhost>
References: <42C68B3A.21290.94FC685@localhost>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.60.0507021256560.19721@localhost>

http://lists.netisland.net/archives/plug/plug-2005-02/msg00216.html

This posting went to the ends of the issue.  To sum up:

Use a package called gtkpod.  It's a GTK gui app for managing tunes on
the ipod.  Use FAT32 formatting on the ipod for now because there's no
fsck utility for HFS+ at this time, though you can hack the HFS+ driver
a bit to work around that.  It's all in the OSX Middleware thread.

Carlos

> nice job -- there are 25 tracks in .ogg format in

As far as I know, no ipods will play .ogg files.  They either have to
be in mp3 or apple's format (a3c/a4c?).  gtkpod is probably running
under this assumption also, and isn't acting on .ogg files.

--Rogan

Subject: [PLUG] iPod with Linux?
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.60.0507021256560.19721@localhost>

On 2 Jul 2005, at 12:59, Carlos Konstanski wrote:

> http://lists.netisland.net/archives/plug/plug-2005-02/msg00216.html
> 
> This posting went to the ends of the issue.  To sum up:
> 
> Use a package called gtkpod.  It's a GTK gui app for managing tunes on
> the ipod.  Use FAT32 formatting on the ipod for now because there's no
> fsck utility for HFS+ at this time, though you can hack the HFS+
> driver a bit to work around that.  It's all in the OSX Middleware
> thread.

I do have gtkpod installed and it runs fine. I just can't get the ogg 
files into the window where they can be selected for installation on 
the iPod. As for the ogg files, I think I read somewhere that gtkpod 
converts the ogg files to mp3s during the transfer to the iPod. If so, 
it doesn't make any sense that it won't allow them into its browser 
window. 

As for the filesystem, from my reading, evidently the iPod comes 
formatted with HFS, which is (?) an Apple filesystem type. If the 
user installs iTunes on a Windows machine, the first time the user 
plugs the iPod into the USB port on the Windows machine iTunes 
senses it, senses that the filesystem is HFS, and automagically 
coverts it to FAT32, preserving the preconfigured data in the 
process. Or maybe it copies the files off to a temp folder, formats 
the whole disk with FAT32, then restores the files. One way or the 
other, the Windows user ends up with a FAT32 iPod.

It seems to me that somewhere I read that there is a Linux tool to 
convert HFS to FAT32, but I don't remember where I got that from. 
I'd prefer not to install iTunes on my Windows computer just so I 
can convert the filesystm. Not only do I not like installing stuff on 
my Windows computer that has the potential to screw something 
up, but I am trying to be a Linux purist here. It's the only way I will 
learn Linux.

But one thing does make sense, now that I think about it. The error 
message when I tried to drag the ogg file to the iPod that said I 
didn't have permission to write to the folder may have had 
something to do with the HFS filesystem. Maybe it's like NTFS -- 
Linux can read it (hence no problem displaying the contents of the 
iPod), but can't write to it. The "permissions" error message may 
have just been a generic error message. OK, that's my currrent 
theory. :)

Thanks for the suggestions. :)


From ckonstan at lunarlogic.com  Sat Jul  2 14:25:29 2005
From: ckonstan at lunarlogic.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Sat, 2 Jul 2005 14:25:29 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] iPod with Linux?
In-Reply-To: <42C69D55.12571.9968155@localhost>
References: <42C68B3A.21290.94FC685@localhost>
	<42C69D55.12571.9968155@localhost>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.60.0507021421350.19721@localhost>

If you dig deep into the OSX Middleware thread, you'll find a patch for
teh HFS+ friver in the 2.6 kernel.  That would allow you to keep the
HFS+ filesystem when using linux.  It disables the disk checking mount
count, basically.

You sure Windows saves the mp3 files off, then reformats, then puts the
files back on?  I thought it simply overwrote the ipod with a FAT32
filesystem and then wrote the os image anew.  How can Windows read the
files without understanding the filesystem?  That's what I'd like to
know.

You cannot use ogg files, as was mentioned.  You must use mp3 files that
have the proper id3 tags.  The ipod will not use the actual filenames.
It will only use the info in the id3 tags.

Carlos

OK, I launched Synaptic and searched on "HFS." That got me nine 
possibilities to install. Some were clearly the wrong thing, but one 
(hfsplus) was described as "tools to access HFS+ formatted 
volumes." I marked it for installation, which prompted Synaptic to 
add libhfsp0, described as "shared library to access HFS+ 
formatted volumes." Unfortunately, after installing them I still can't 
write to the iPod. 

However, I did just discover that I can read and copy from it. I never 
tried that before, so I don't know if the tools I just installed made it 
possible. All I know is that I copied the three folders (Contacts, 
Calendars, iPod_Control and Notes) into my /home directory. 
Nevertheless, there are three files listed (.Trashes, ,Journal and 
.jpurnal_info_block) that I cannot copy. All three have an X in the 
file icon. When I try to copy them I get the "permissions" error 
message.

Actually, I don't give a rat's patootie if the iPod retains HFS or is 
converted to FAT32. I just want something I can read and write to, 
and I don't want to destroy the system Apple installed to enable 
me to play the tracks from the menu.

> You sure Windows saves the mp3 files off, then reformats, then puts
> the files back on?  I thought it simply overwrote the ipod with a
> FAT32 filesystem and then wrote the os image anew.  How can Windows
> read the files without understanding the filesystem?  That's what I'd
> like to know.

Maybe Windows doesn't understand it, but iTunes does. I have no 
idea what I'm talking about. You're probably right. More likely 
iTunes installs the correct files on the Windows computer, formats 
the iPod as FAT32 -- wiping everything out -- then transfers the files 
to it.

> You cannot use ogg files, as was mentioned.  You must use mp3 files
> that have the proper id3 tags.  The ipod will not use the actual
> filenames. It will only use the info in the id3 tags.

Somewhere I know I read that one of these utilities (gtkpod, I think) 
converts the ogg files to mp3s as it transfers them to the iPod.

But perhaps that is not correct. I put the Stravinksy CD back in the 
drive and decided to re-rip it. This time I went into Config in grip and 
found a number of options --

bladeenc
lame
l3enc
xingmp3enc
gogo
oggenc
flac
other

It was set on oggenc, so I switched it to mp3encode. When I did 
the "encode file extension" box changed from ogg to mp3. "Cool," I 
thought, and proceeded to start the rip process. But when I tried to 
start it I got an error message "invalid encoder executable, Check 
your encoder config." Looking at the encoder config there are a 
bazillion settings that can be changed. And I don't know what any 
of them mean. :(

So for the moment I'm stymied. Wish grip had a help file to explain 
the settings.

> found a number of options --
> 
> bladeenc
> lame
> l3enc
> xingmp3enc
> gogo

> flac
> other

> start it I got an error message "invalid encoder executable, Check 
> your encoder config." Looking at the encoder config there are a 
> bazillion settings that can be changed. And I don't know what any 
> of them mean. :(

> the settings.

grip is just a front-end for command-line mp3 (ogg, etc) encoders.  The 
list you see is the various encoders it knows how to talk to, so you 
should pick one and then make sure it's installed.  Lame is pretty 
common I think.

Jason


From ckonstan at lunarlogic.com  Sat Jul  2 19:49:54 2005
From: ckonstan at lunarlogic.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Sat, 2 Jul 2005 19:49:54 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] iPod with Linux?
In-Reply-To: <42C6D324.12546.A68B122@localhost>
References: <42C69D55.12571.9968155@localhost>
	<42C6D324.12546.A68B122@localhost>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.60.0507021945550.19721@localhost>

There's one more variable to this equation.  The kernel needs support
for the filesystem.  Userland programs are not enough.  You may want to
fire up "make menuconfig" and browse your kernel's filesystem settings.
If HFS+ is there, but compiled as a module, you'll need to modprobe it
or add it to /etc/modules.

But I really think it's best to just use FAT32 for simplicity's sake.
You won't lose the ipod OS if you follow the instructions in this
writeup:

http://pag.csail.mit.edu/~adonovan/hacks/ipod.html

There's a step where you dd the OS into a file, and then dd it back
after the reformat.  Scary, but it works fine.

Carlos

On Sat, 2 Jul 2005, John Jordan wrote:
> On 2 Jul 2005, at 14:25, Carlos Konstanski wrote:
>
>> If you dig deep into the OSX Middleware thread, you'll find a patch for 
>> teh HFS+ friver in the 2.6 kernel.  That would allow you to keep the 
>> HFS+ filesystem when using linux.  It disables the disk checking mount 
>> count, basically.
>
> OK, I launched Synaptic and searched on "HFS." That got me nine 
> possibilities to install. Some were clearly the wrong thing, but one 
> (hfsplus) was described as "tools to access HFS+ formatted volumes." I 
> marked it for installation, which prompted Synaptic to add libhfsp0, 
> described as "shared library to access HFS+ formatted volumes."

OK, if you want to go that route, then go ahead.  You'll need kernel 
support for HFS as well as adding the ability to read Apple hard disk 
partitions.

[This might be irrelevant -- read ahead before acting]
However, I think that's a mistake.  Microsoft does a good job of making 
its stuff "standard" by not supporting any thing else.  So if you want to 
be compatible, you gotta do it their way.

For an ipod, you're probably going to want to reformat the thing VFAT. 
The reason to do this is simple:  If you EVER want to use the ipod as a 
portable hard disk, you might want to put the data on a Windows PC or even 
a Linux box that doesn't have all the special Apple stuff installed.  Odds 
are pretty good it has support for Winders filesystems, so if you go that 
route, you're more universally adapted.

To do this, you need to actually use a Windows machine (I don't know of 
any other way) and install the "ipod updater" from support.apple.com.  It 
will put the latest OS on the ipod and transparently reformat the disk 
VFAT.

This should take half the headache out of connecting the ipod to your 
Ubuntu machine, also.
[End of probably irrelevant section -- seems this has been done]

> However, I did just discover that I can read and copy from it. I never 
> tried that before, so I don't know if the tools I just installed made it 
> possible.

Likely, connecting the system to iTunes on Windows ran the updater or 
something similar and already converted it to VFAT.

> All I know is that I copied the three folders (Contacts, Calendars, 
> iPod_Control and Notes) into my /home directory. Nevertheless, there are 
> three files listed (.Trashes, ,Journal and .jpurnal_info_block) that I 
> cannot copy. All three have an X in the file icon. When I try to copy 
> them I get the "permissions" error message.

Who mounted the filesystem and with what permissions?

We'll get to doing that automatically in a second.

> Actually, I don't give a rat's patootie if the iPod retains HFS or is 
> converted to FAT32. I just want something I can read and write to, and I 
> don't want to destroy the system Apple installed to enable me to play 
> the tracks from the menu.

Changing the thing to VFAT doesn't lose you any functionality whatsoever.

> Maybe Windows doesn't understand it, but iTunes does. I have no idea 
> what I'm talking about. You're probably right. More likely iTunes 
> installs the correct files on the Windows computer, formats the iPod as 
> FAT32 -- wiping everything out -- then transfers the files to it.

That's exactly what it does.

A word of warning, gtkpod's database is a little sensitive.  I've had 
weird little problems using both gtkpod and iTunes (iTunes on other 
people's computers, of course).  The data gets munged and I have to do a 
whole lot of manual moving around in order to restore the database when I 
want to syncronize my ipod with my desktop.  Basically, I every time I 
connect my ipod to a non-gtkpod system, I have to wipe all my files on the 
ipod and put them back again.  It's a pain, but I just do it before bed 
and it's fine.

If you want to know whether or not this was done, when the ipod is 
connected to your Linux system and you can read the filesystem, type 
"mount" at a command prompt and find the ipod listing.  It might be 
"/dev/ipod" or maybe "/dev/sda3" or something... anyway, it'll be mounted 
in the place where you read the ipod files.  See what is listed under 
"type".  That'll tell you immediately.

>> You cannot use ogg files, as was mentioned.  You must use mp3 files 
>> that have the proper id3 tags.  The ipod will not use the actual 
>> filenames. It will only use the info in the id3 tags.
>
> Somewhere I know I read that one of these utilities (gtkpod, I think) 
> converts the ogg files to mp3s as it transfers them to the iPod.

I suppose that's possible, but it's a needless step.  If you're resigning 
yourself to using MP3 at all (as you must on the ipod), then go ahead and 
just rip 'em as MP3.

> But perhaps that is not correct. I put the Stravinksy CD back in the 
> drive and decided to re-rip it. This time I went into Config in grip and 
> found a number of options --
>
> bladeenc
> lame
> l3enc
> xingmp3enc
> gogo
> oggenc
> flac
> other
>
> It was set on oggenc, so I switched it to mp3encode.

Personally, I'd go with lame.

> When I did the "encode file extension" box changed from ogg to mp3. 
> "Cool," I thought, and proceeded to start the rip process. But when I 
> tried to start it I got an error message "invalid encoder executable, 
> Check your encoder config." Looking at the encoder config there are a 
> bazillion settings that can be changed. And I don't know what any of 
> them mean. :(

Make sure you actually have the encoder installed.

For Debian, I use the rarewares, xmixahlx, and Marillat's archive for 
multimedia stuff:

deb http://rarewares.org/debian/packages/unstable/ ./
deb http://download.videolan.org/pub/videolan/debian sid main
deb http://debian.xmixahlx.com/packages/unstable/ ./

If you care to use these, you might want some entries in /etc/apt/apt.conf 
to clear up possible collisions.  I'll share that with anybody who's 
interested.

> So for the moment I'm stymied. Wish grip had a help file to explain the 
> settings.

grip DOES have fairly extensive help files.  They're HTML pages and, at 
least on my system, they're accessible from the Help menu.

Now, the real trick for getting the ipod to do things perfectly on my 
system was the creation of /dev/ipod and point it in the right place. 
This was done with udev and autofs.  If you need help with that part, let 
me know.

If gtkpod can "sync" with the ipod and doesn't give any errors when 
writing the appropriate filesystem to the ipod, you're probably in good 
shape.

I also have to "eject" my ipod after each use in order to get the SCSI 
subsystem to release the thing and take the ipod out of drive mode.

J.

Nope!  There's actually a menu option under "File" in gtkpod to create the

> From what I gather I can fix that problem if I load iTunes on my Windows

You don't need all of iTunes, just the ipod updater.  It's available as a

> HFS+ to FAT32 filesystem conversion utilities.

Um, no.  Part of the ipod software is on the filesystem and part is in 
firmware.  If you go the hand-conversion route, you could seriously fuck 
things up.

I think it's already VFAT.  Check that first.  If not, use the ipod 
updater on Windows.

>OK, I just determined that it's mounted. It's at /media/iPod/. How

mount should tell you that

  mount

or (to see just the ipod)

  mount | grep iPod

You should see something like:
<device> on <mountpoint> type <type> (<mountopts>)

<mountopts> had better be rw

From ewilhelm at sbcglobal.net  Sat Jul  2 21:28:26 2005
From: ewilhelm at sbcglobal.net (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Sat, 2 Jul 2005 21:28:26 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] iPod with Linux?
In-Reply-To: <42C6FFAF.29137.B16B087@localhost>
References: <42C6F00F.25494.AD9A65E@localhost>
	<42C6FFAF.29137.B16B087@localhost>
Message-ID: <200507022128.27008.ewilhelm@sbcglobal.net>

# The following was supposedly scribed by
# John Jordan
# on Saturday 02 July 2005 08:57 pm:

>So now I'm wondering if that "you don't have permission" error
>message was for real, not just a generic message for a write
>failure. I am logged in as jjj, not as root.

ls -ld /media/iPod

ls -l /media/iPod

>I just tried to chmod the iPod, but I have forgotten the chmod
>syntax. I tried sudo chmod *.* jjj. 

chmod --help
man chmod
info chmod

>Wrong, eh? Hey, when you're a 
>beginner you have to guess a lot! :(

Don't guess.  You can really hose things doing that.  It's best if you 
learn to read the manpages and info docs.  Also, apropos is a good 
place to start if you're trying to figure out what does what.

I've always found it amusing that you have to tell the shell that you're 
--help | less when you're a newbie :-)

  <command> --help | less

Some commands don't dump their help on stdout, in which case you have to 
redirect the stderr to read the whole message.

  <command> --help 2>&1 | less

But of course with most modern terminals, you can just use Shift+PgUp/ 
Shift+PgDn to scroll through the history.

Some programs (like the interpreter for something that ends in "ython") 
will unhelpfully tell you that --help doesn't work and you have to use 
a -h switch.  Others will just rudely create files named ./--help.  
Sorry to sprinkle so many caveats about, but at least most of the core 
utilities are well-behaved.

--Eric

On Sat, 2 Jul 2005, John Jordan wrote:
> Yep, it is. It says:
>
> /dev/sda3 on /media/iPod type hfsplus
> (rw,nosuid,nodev,uid=1000,gid=1000)
>
> And when I did modprobe hfsplus I didn't get any error messages. So I 
> must have HFS+ support.

Sure.  But I still recommend making the thing VFAT for the aforementioned 
portability issues.  It's really nice to use your ipod as a portable file 
storage device.

> So it must be a permissions thing. Back to researching chmod
> syntax. :)

Nope.  You need to research modifying your automounter to set the 
permissions properly when it attaches the device.

You probably just need to add a "user" option to the entry in 
/etc/auto.removable (or /etc/auto.something).

OK, this sounds like sensible advice. I started by looking through 
/etc, but I didn't see anything that looked likely to be this file. Then 
I did a search of the whole filesystem for *auto*.*. That brought up 
122 files, but none looked like it. I also searched for *mount*.*, 
which turned up hundreds of files. But again, nothing looked likely. 
Then I decided I'd just hit Help or Google, but I don't know what I'm 
really looking for. And the Help files included with Ubuntu 5.04 are 
not very complete, and pretty lame when it comes to searching. So 
I guess I need a little more direction here.

Now, as to the other issue -- using the iPod as a removable 
storage device. While searching for the "auto" file I ran into 
hosts.deny and hosts.accept. That reminded me that there is a 
problem wrt to the Windows computer. To clarify, I have a Windows 
2000 desktop plus the Linux laptop. Both are on home ethernet (as 
are my Laserjets). No wireless at home. The desktop is Devil4 and 
the Linux laptop is Devil5.

The laptop has no problem seeing and accessing the two shares I 
created on the Windows computer. It prompts for my password on 
the Windows computer, and then I can move, delete, write, etc. 
anything I want on those shares. 

But going the other direction isn't working. The Windows computer 
sees Devil5, but when I click on it to see the share I created on it 
(/home) I get a prompt for the password to get into Devil5. The box 
has an entry for username and another for the password, but no 
matter what I put in them I can't get in. This has been something 
I've been meaning to fix for some time, but I put it on the back 
burner for the time being. As long as the Linux computer can get to 
the Windows shares I can just do everything from the Linux 
computer. But it would be nice to figure out why the Linux laptop is 
refusing to accept my username and password from the Windows 
computer.

The other issue is something I have never tried to do before. Now 
that I have a laptop and will be using it from other locations, I have 
been thinking about remote login to the Windows computer at 
home. The Windows desktop is on 24/7 and is connected by cable 
and router. I know Windows has a remote login feature, but I have 
never bothered to figure it out. I just left it turned off and ignored it. 
What I am thinking is that this would be way better than trying to 
use the iPod as a removable file storage device. If I can log into my 
desktop from the university I can just move files back and forth as I 
need to. Plus it would be handy when I forget and leave a 
homework file on the Windows computer that I need at school. I'd 
rather work on getting remote login going than worry about 
converting the iPod to VFAT. Besides, from what I understand, 
VFAT is still basically FAT, subject to corruption and stuff. Then 
again, I don't know how good a file system HFS+ is. But just the 
fact that it was not created by Microsoft means it has to be better 
right? :)

Another reason for leaving the iPod as HFS+ is that I am way less 
likely to muck it up. 

So, after considering everything, I think I would prefer to leave it 
HFS+. Now I just need to figure out which file is the automounter 
permissions file and how to modify it.

On 3 Jul 2005, at 9:13, John Jordan wrote:

Followup --

In re-reading:

http://pag.csail.mit.edu/~adonovan/hacks/ipod.html#hfs

I discovered the following:

-----
Eric also provided the following tip for mounting his HFS+ iPod in 
read/write mode:

    I had been having a hell of a time getting HFS+ to mount 
read/write. Mounting via "mount -t hfsplus " only let me mount read-
only (useless for then attempting to place files on your iPod). After 
some thrashing about, and trying to deal with the spurious 
message that appeared in /var/log/kern.log about hfs+ not having 
been unmounted cleanly, I happened upon the command 
"hpmount" which works just like regular mount except it is for 
hfsplus filesystems.

    Now I just use "hpmount /dev/sde3" (sde3 is my iPod) and I'm 
good to go.

    Mind you I'm a Debian (Sarge) user. YMMV for other distros.
-----

So I decided to give that a try. From a regular terminal, here is 
what happened:

jjj at Devil5:/$ hpmount /dev/sda3
*** Warning: You are about to open '/dev/sda3' for writing ***
*** Do you really want to do that? (y/n) ***
y
hpmount: /dev/sda3: error opening medium (Permission denied)
jjj at Devil5:/$ sudo hpmount /dev/sda3
*** Warning: You are about to open '/dev/sda3' for writing ***
*** Do you really want to do that? (y/n) ***
y
hpmount: /dev/sda3: no error (Success)

So then I went back to gtkpod and tried again. Still no success. 
Same error messages.

Remembering a trick, I closed gtkpod and then launched it from the 
terminal with "sudo gtkpod." When it came up I tried again. Still no 
luck.

Maybe someone has additional suggestions. But I know I'm almost 
there!

Try looking at your packages and documentation before your filesystem.

apt-cache search automount
apt-cache search mount | grep -i auto

(then look to see which of those packages is installed)

Also try apropos

  apropos mount

Some of the man pages actually tell you about configuration files (the 
ones in section 5.)  Sorry I don't know a more elegant way than this:

  apropos '' | grep '(5)'

This I may have actually understood part of. :)

Opening fstab in gedit it says:

# /etc/fstab: static file system information.
#
[pretend the items below are tabbed -- I can't make it work in e-mail]
# <file system> <mount point> <type> <options> <dump> <pass>
proc /proc proc defaults 0 0
/dev/hda2 / ext3 defaults, errors=remount -ro 0
/dev/hda1 none swap sw 0 0
/dev/hdc /media/cdrom0 udf,iso9660 ro,user,noauto 0
/dev/sda /media/usb0 auto rw,user,noauto 0 0

I don't know what the proc line is all about, but I do know hda1 and 
hda2, because I created them when I installed Ubuntu. And 
/dev/hdc is pretty obvious. And I think I just learned something -- 
the "ro" and "rw" mean "read-only" and "read-write," correct? And 
in hda2 where it says "-ro" that means "not read-only"? And "user" 
means any user has full access?

It took me a moment, but I also figured out /dev/sda/media/usb0. 
At one time I had my external USB hard disk connected directly to 
this Linux laptop. At the time it was still FAT32, which is how 
Seagate shipped it. It is now NTFS and hanging on the Windows 
2000 computer, where it is going to stay. I created a share on the 
Windows 2000 computer and the Linux laptop reads and writes to 
it now through the share. Evidently once an entry is made in fstab 
it stays there, even if the drive goes away. But I think that line 
could be deleted, since I have no intention of putting it back on the 
laptop.

Reading about iPods and Linux, it appears Apple creates three 
partitions. Sda1 is just a few bytes long. Sda2 is a bit larger and is 
some kind of filesystem map. And sda3 is where all the rest of the 
stuff hangs. 

So (guessing ahead here), if I were to add a line that says 
something like:

/dev/sda3 /media/iPod auto? rw,usr,noauto? 0 0

I might solve the problem? (Except some of what I put in that line 
may be wrong [note question marks], or missing.) I'm scared to 
mess with it, lest I end up with a computer that can't find its 
filesystem.

From athlonrob at axpr.net  Sun Jul  3 16:12:20 2005

John Jordan wrote:
> I tried man touch, but it just reinforced my low opinion of man 
> pages. I did get an idea of what touch can do, but I could only read 
> the first 22 pages of 65. That's because that's as far as it would 
> scroll. At the bottom it said "22/65" End", highlighted. I tried every 
> key combination I could think of to make it go further, but since it 
> didn't give me the secret code, I just gave up and closed the 
> terminal window. I guess when they wrote the man pages they 
> assumed we were all born knowing what the key combination to 
> scroll further is. :(

Actually that 22/65 means you were viewing lines 22 through 65 and (END)
means you're at the end of the man page.

Quick keys for man:

space or page down -> page down
page up -> page up
enter -> line down
up arrow -> line up
down arrow -> line down
left arrow -> scroll left
right arrow -> scroll right
end -> go to the end of the man page
home -> go to the beginning of the man page
q -> quit
/ -> search (/foo<enter> takes you to the first instance of foo.
/<enter> will then take you to the next instance of foo)

Uhhh... I'm not sure what other ones you might use... those pretty much
cover it for me.  :-)

Rob


From ewilhelm at sbcglobal.net  Sun Jul  3 16:19:02 2005
From: ewilhelm at sbcglobal.net (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Sun, 3 Jul 2005 16:19:02 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] iPod with Linux?
In-Reply-To: <42C8024E.24338.F08EC47@localhost>
References: <42C7B688.11818.DE0EEF6@localhost>
	<42C8024E.24338.F08EC47@localhost>
Message-ID: <200507031619.02438.ewilhelm@sbcglobal.net>

# The following was supposedly scribed by
# John Jordan
# on Sunday 03 July 2005 03:20 pm:

>/dev/sda3 /media/iPod auto? rw,usr,noauto? 0 0

Getting close.  You probably want an explicit type, though I bet you 
could get away with auto.  This should be the type that you get out of 
the mount command when it is mounted.

make that "user", not usr, and noauto means "don't do it at boot", which 
is probably correct.  Now check "man fstab"

/dev/sda3 /media/iPod auto rw,user,noauto 0 0

I'm not sure about your automounter.  You may need to disable it.  In 
any case, once you have a listing in fstab, all you have to do is 
"mount /media/iPod" and "umount /media/iPod" (make sure you umount 
before you unplug it (that flushes everything that's hanging around in 
ram pretending to have been written to disk to the actual disk so that 
it goes with you when you unplug it.))

--Eric

Uh oh. It may be hosed. FAT32 may be on the horizon. :(

Here is what I did:

1) Removed the USB drive line from fstab and added the line for the 
iPod. Saved it.

2) Tried to add tracks via gtkpod. Still no go. (Didn't expect it to 
without remounting, but worth a try.)

3) I had already read about the necessity of unmounting before 
unplugging, so I did just as you said -- umount /media/iPod. The 
process went OK and no error messages.

4) Unplugged the iPod from the USB.

5) Plugged it back in. I have a folder in the panel to view it, so I 
launched to folder. It opened a window, which suddenly blinked and 
then displayed the fact that it was unable to view anything. While 
pondering this, suddenly the iPod came up. I guess USB takes a 
moment to initialize. Or maybe it was waiting on the HD in the iPod.

But first inkling of bad news. The window used to show:

Calendars
Contacts
Notes
iPod_Control
     Device (empty folder)
     Music
          20 empty folders here, labeled F00 - F19

Now it shows:

Desktop DB
Desktop DF
Finder
ReadMe
System

And in the browser window Nautilus has no idea what these are. 
They are displayed with the little Gnome icon, not a folder or a file 
icon. I tried to open the ReadMe in gedit, but no go.

Very strange. What happened to the other structure? Did I hose it 
when I unplugged it? No, from that site it said unplugging without 
first unmounting caused a corrupted file system. This is not 
corrupted. It is just completely different. I even took Nautilus all the

No, that's probably your automounter seeing the new device and mounting 
it.  Do (as root) "tail -f /var/log/messages" while you plug it in and 
you might see what the automounter is doing (probably starts with 
something about hotplug sda<num> and etc.)

As for the directory structure looking different, what is in 
your /etc/fstab now?  (btw, you didn't really need to remove the usb 
line (but for the sake of simplicity, you could comment (start the line 
with #) it out.)

Does the "hfsplus" driver usually call itself HFS-fs in the kernel?  I

taking precidence.

where you say "auto".  Then re-mount the thing.

appears there.  Watch dmesg or /var/log/messages for anything funky
happening.

From athlonrob at axpr.net  Sun Jul  3 23:35:37 2005
From: athlonrob at axpr.net (AthlonRob)
Date: Sun, 03 Jul 2005 23:35:37 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] iPod with Linux?
In-Reply-To: <42C85D7D.27155.106D1D08@localhost>
References: <42C851E1.4768.103FC444@localhost>
	<42C85D7D.27155.106D1D08@localhost>
Message-ID: <42C8D8B9.2030709@axpr.net>

John Jordan wrote:
> I did tail -f /var/log/messages, then unmounted, unplugged, and 
> plugged it back in. The messages were identical to what I sent 
> earlier, that is, the last line still says "localhost kernel: HFS-fs: 
> Filesystem is marked locked, mounting read only."

Okay, I'm relatively certain now you *are* using the proper driver.  I'm
also relatively certain the filesystem being marked locked is the issue
at hand.... or at least *one* issue at hand.  :-)

I have absolutely zero experience with HFS and what utilities are
available for it, and my quick google search didn't turn up much of use.
 You may need to google some more.

If you dig in to the kernel source, you'll see in fs/hfs/mdb.c:
        if ((attrib & cpu_to_be16(HFS_SB_ATTRIB_SLOCK))) {
                hfs_warn("HFS-fs: Filesystem is marked locked, mounting
read-only.\n");
                sb->s_flags |= MS_RDONLY;
        }

...which is where our issue sits.  That if is happening.  I'm guessing
HFS_SB_ATTRIB_SLOCK is true here.  So it seems there's some sort of a
property an HFS filesystem can have that "soft locks" it.  How do we
un-lock it?  I don't know.  That's where Google should come in and help.
 :-)

Oh, a thought just occured to me.  What are the odds you're mounting the
OS partition (which would likely be marked read-only) instead of the
data/music partition on the iPod?  No, wait, the partition you're
mounting was reported to be 60GB in size, wasn't it?

While I wouldn't recommend it, you could potentially modify the kernel
source, commenting out that if block, and see what happens from there.
If that soft lock is just one of those "save the user from himself"
locks I say throw it away.  :-)

I wonder if other pluggers here have experience with HFS?  Maybe
somebody could shed some light on this soft lock issue?

Rob

From creswick at gmail.com  Mon Jul  4 09:16:34 2005
From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)

On 7/4/05, John Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> wrote:
> 
> While googling I found a page that said that gtkpod would read the
> files on the iPod if you click on the Read button. Not sure I tried
> that before. I did just now, with the iPod mounted. It said:
> 
> /media/iPod/iPod_Contol/iTunes/iTunesDB does not exist. Import
> aborted.

This *really* sounds like you have the wrong partition mounted. 
Before blowing everything away, you may want to try sda1 and 2 first..
(apologies if you already have, I missed out on a section of this
thread).

FWIW, HFS is also agnostic to case.  It will maintain upper case
characters, but it dosen't consider iPod to be different from ipod. 
Now, /media/iPod *is* different from /media/ipod, as you correctly
assumed (because it is the mount point, it is on your linux
filesystem, not the iPod's file system).

> So now I need to figure out if Linux can format the iPod as FAT32.
> (I still don't want to install any part of iTunes on my Windows
> computer, if I can avoid it.)

When I was working with my iPod, I found a tool that did exactly this
(I don't recall the name, though :( ).  I don't think you want to try
and do it manually so to speak, with standard formatting tools.  Your
iPod apparently has a non-trivial partitioning scheme, and changing
the wrong partition could be very difficult to undo.

Good luck, and good job.

--Rogan

> as just formatting it FAT32... you have to deal with the software on
> the iPod itself, remember.

OK, I gave up. Someone else had posted earlier that you didn't 
have to install the whole iTunes on your Windows computer to 
format the iPod, so I decided I'd just do it. I put the Apple CD in the 
drive and the installation utility came up. After getting past the 
license stuff it asked if I wanted to continue without checking to 
see if my iPod needed to be formatted. If I wanted to check I was 
to plug it in.

So I connected the iPod to an old USB 1.1 port (I have a 2.0 card, 
but I'd have to crawl around on the floor behind the computer). 
Windows popped up the usual "new device found." And then the 
Apple installation utility announced that it needed to be formatted. I 
told it to go ahead. To make a long story short, it formatted it and 
put all the correct files and database stuff on it. Then it prompted 
me to continue with the install. I aborted at that point, unplugged 
the iPod and plugged it into my Linux laptop.

However, I had to change the entry in fstab from sda3 to sda2 and 
the filesystem from HFS to VFAT before it would mount. As soon 
as it was mounted (I just did "mount /dev/sda2") Nautilus popped 
up a window showing the folders -- and all the folders were restored 
the way they had been previously.

So then I launched gtkpod. I added the 25 Stravinsky tracks and 
told it to sync with the iPod. This seemed to work -- no error 
messages, unlike previously. But after I unmounted the iPod and 
looked, nothing was there.

I spent the next ten minutes poking around trying to get the files 
onto the iPod, and I finally succeeded. Interestingly, the iPod menu 
just displays Do Not Disconnect continuously until I unmount it. In 
other words, I have to unmount it in order to see if the tracks made 
it to the iPod.

I was delighted to see that they were there. I plugged in the Apple 
earbuds and navigated through the music menu to the tracks. I 
selected one and pushed what looked like a Play button. At that 
point the iPod popped up a progress bar and appeared to start 
playing the track, except it took it all of two seconds to play it, and 
then it went on to the next track. And the next track and the next 
track all the way through all 25.

It appeared that the tracks were added to the database but perhaps 
the actual music files didn't make it. Being unsure, I remounted it 
and went back to gtkpod. Then I decided to delete all the tracks 
from the iPod and start over. Deleting them went OK, and it 
appeared to reinstall them but, once again, all it does is cycle 
through the tracks quickly without playing anything.

I suspect the problem is twofold:

1) I have no idea what the menu items and buttons on gtkpod are 
referring to. As I said before, my idea of playing music is to slap a 
CD in the player and go sit down in my LazyBoy. If it has more 
than an on-off switch and a volume control I have to call in the 
neighbor's teenager.

2) I have no idea how the iPod is supposed to work and which 
buttons make it go. Plus, the much vaunted click wheel is a PITA. 
It's sort of like a touchpad on a laptop. First it's not sensitive 
enough and then suddenly you've scrolled all the way to the end of 
the menu choices. And just as you click the center button to 
choose the menu item you have highlighted, the highlight shifts to 
an adjacent menu item and you're off somewhere you didn't want to 
go. Oh well. All I will have on it is classical, so I won't have to fiddle 
with it more than once per hour. :)

As a further possibility, maybe when I ripped the CD with grip the 
other day I screwed up that process. They may say they are mp3s, 
but are they really?

Anyway, just wanted you guys who have been helping me to know 
that the HFS+ filesystem is no longer an issue. I still have issues, 
though. I will probably always have issues. :)

Thanks for your comments and help. 

So I guess my next step is to try to figure them out.

From ali.corbin at gmail.com  Wed Jul  6 09:16:45 2005
From: ali.corbin at gmail.com (Ali Corbin)
Date: Wed, 6 Jul 2005 09:16:45 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Running LaTeX Within Emacs
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0507060851290.15995@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0507060851290.15995@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <965f90c2050706091622864b6@mail.gmail.com>

On 7/6/05, Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:
>    If I'm creating/editing a .tex file within emacs I can run TeX on it from a
> menu, but that is not what I want to do. I want to run latex on the source
> file. Is there a way to do this?
> 
>    Or, do I save the file then run latex from a virtual console?
> 

I don't do this myself, but I just took a look at the latex section of
the emacs wiki:

    http://www.emacswiki.org/cgi-bin/wiki/LaTeX

and it appears that the .tex suffix by default puts you in tex mode. 
You can try renaming your files to .latex or .ltx, or do a M-x
latex-mode in the .tex file to see if it works any better.  (You can
also use auto-mode-alist to force .tex files to be put into latex
mode.)

Ali

On 7/6/05, Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:
>    If I'm creating/editing a .tex file within emacs I can run TeX on it from a
> menu, but that is not what I want to do. I want to run latex on the source
> file. Is there a way to do this?

For projects of any reasonable size, or anything with a bibliography,
I recommend building a Makefile.  Once that is done, you can use M-x
compile to latex your file/project (bound to f5 for me, but I'm not
sure that's standard).

Subject: [PLUG] NFS Question

I haven't been using nfs is quite some time, and Linux/nfs has improved
significantly (At least, people say it has.).

In any case, what parameters should I use on the server side to get
optimal performance without losing data.  At the moment, the only thing 
I'm doing is to use:

/home/            server01.DavidMandel.com(rw,setuid,no_root_squash)

in /etc/exports on the server and 

server01.DavidMandel.com:/home /home nfs 
                   rsize=8192,wsize=8192,soft,intr,bg,tcp

in /etc/fstab on the client side.

This seems to work, but I haven't tested it very well yet and I'm 
not at all sure the rsize and wsize are correct and/or needed.
I'm including them mostly because they improved performance when
I was using non-Linux servers.

Anyone have a recommendation that could save me time on 
experimentation?

From heinlein at madboa.com  Wed Jul  6 11:26:10 2005
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Wed, 06 Jul 2005 11:26:10 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] NFS Question
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.58.0507061051450.13811@PPC.DavidMandel.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.58.0507061051450.13811@PPC.DavidMandel.com>
Message-ID: <42CC2242.7050603@madboa.com>

On 7/6/05 11:10 AM, David Mandel wrote:
> I haven't been using nfs is quite some time, and Linux/nfs has improved
> significantly (At least, people say it has.).
> 
> In any case, what parameters should I use on the server side to get
> optimal performance without losing data.  At the moment, the only thing 
> I'm doing is to use:
> 
> /home/            server01.DavidMandel.com(rw,setuid,no_root_squash)

I think the "sync" option is now the default, but you may want to make 
it explicit to avoid server-side warning messages.

> in /etc/exports on the server and 
> 
> server01.DavidMandel.com:/home /home nfs 
>                    rsize=8192,wsize=8192,soft,intr,bg,tcp

The NFS maintainers recommend against the "soft" option since it can 
result in invisible data corruption. See section 4.3.1 of the NFS HOW-TO:

* http://nfs.sourceforge.net/nfs-howto/client.html

Since you're using TCP (ergo NFS v3 or higher) for transport, you 
probably don't need to specify rsize and wsize; the server will specify 
the default block size. I think the default for NFS over TCP is 32K 
blocks, but that may have changed of late.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

From athlonrob at axpr.net  Wed Jul  6 17:57:56 2005
From: athlonrob at axpr.net (AthlonRob)
Date: Wed, 06 Jul 2005 17:57:56 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Memory configuration
In-Reply-To: <20050706192849.21978.qmail@web50601.mail.yahoo.com>
References: <20050706192849.21978.qmail@web50601.mail.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <42CC7E14.8000905@axpr.net>

Robert Kopp wrote:
> My memory isn't quite right. I have an Asus A7N8X-E
> deluxe (socket A) and two single-channel PC2700
> sticks, one 256MB and one 512MB, in the two blue
> slots. These sticks are pretty generic. The board
> supports dual-channel and up to PC3200. What would you
> do instead of what I already have?

What CPU do you have?  Odds are you won't see much, if any, speed boost
from going to PC3200.  Additionally, real-world speed gains from going
dual-channel are minimal outside of a few specific applications.
SETI at Home *really* likes dual-channelness and everything likes it if
you're using integrated video (I don't even know if the -E includes the
integrated video or not, however).

To go dual-channel you need reasonably well matched memory (I wouldn't
suggest PC2700 and PC3200 - and really this is debatable, it should go
lowest-common-denominator on you) and you need the same amount of RAM in
each bank.  If you currently have a 512MB stick and a 256MB stick, you
can go dual-channel by putting the 512MB stick in the slot by itself and
an additional 256MB stick in the remaining slot, up next to your
existing 256MB slot.  Bam, you should have dual-channel goodness.  :-)

This *should* be outlined in your motherboard manual to some extent...
although those things are generally quite horribly written.

Rob

From athlonrob at axpr.net  Thu Jul  7 18:55:41 2005
From: athlonrob at axpr.net (AthlonRob)
Date: Thu, 07 Jul 2005 18:55:41 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Memory configuration
In-Reply-To: <20050707213739.525.qmail@web50606.mail.yahoo.com>
References: <20050707213739.525.qmail@web50606.mail.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <42CDDD1D.4050004@axpr.net>

Robert Kopp wrote:
> I have an XP 2500+. The 512MB module is toast; after
> removing it I have 256MB of PC2700 in the middle slot.

Ouch.  Toasted RAM chips are never good.

> If I wanted to upgrade RAM, what would be the best
> path, considering the (possible) use of the 256MB module?

"Best" ??

Go buy yourself two 1GB PC3200 chips and chuck the 256MB module.

Next best would be to get another 512MB chip and another 256MB chip and
arrange them as I described earlier for dual-channel goodness.

Next best would be to get another 256MB chip and arrange it so they
aren't on the same bank.

Next best after that would be to get two 128MB chips and put them in the
two-slot bank and move the 256MB chip so it's in its own bank.

Rob


From znmeb at cesmail.net  Thu Jul  7 19:38:55 2005
From: znmeb at cesmail.net (M. Edward (Ed) Borasky)
Date: Thu, 07 Jul 2005 19:38:55 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Have I slain my windows partition?
In-Reply-To: <20050707144036.GA32395@sccrmxc13.comcast.net>
References: <20050707144036.GA32395@sccrmxc13.comcast.net>
Message-ID: <42CDE73F.2030705@cesmail.net>

Yes, you probably have slain Windows. Parted is flaky at worst; at best
it will refuse to do destructive things. Here's what I'd recommend:

1. Get a legitimate Windows bootable CD of the same release as what was
on the hard drive. It should have an option to repair Windows. Unless
you managed to trash the filesystem beyond repair, you should be able to
get Windows back up and running.

2. If you do get Windows up and running, boot it up and see what it says
about the hard drive partitioning. Best case is, in addition to the main
Windows partition, you either have a live Linux partition or free space.
If you did a successful Linux mkfs on the live partition, Windows will
notice it's there but you won't be able to do anything with it. Back up
whatever you can from the NTFS filesystem. If you can't get Windows up,
boot Knoppix again, do the backup of the NTFS filesystem using Knoppix
with the NTFS filesystem mounted read-only.

3. If Windows is slain, decide whether you want it back and re-install
if you do. During the re-install, you'll get an opportunity to set aside
some free space for Linux. If you don't want Windows back, just nuke the
hard drive and install Linux.

In general, despite what the Linux hackers say, Knoppix and other
Linux-based toolsets aren't really cut out to "rescue" Windows systems.
About the only thing you can *reliably* do with them is mount an NTFS
partition read-only and copy files from it to another partition, usually
FAT32. For that matter, FAT32 partitions made by a Windows system
usually work reliably in Linux, but the converse isn't true -- FAT32
partitions made by Linux don't necessarily come up in Windows.


>  
>


From michael at mcmm.net  Thu Jul  7 20:01:04 2005
From: michael at mcmm.net (Michael Chan)
Date: Thu, 07 Jul 2005 20:01:04 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] A minor visual configuration question
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0506271350200.10633@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0506271350200.10633@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <1120791664.24017.12.camel@localhost.localdomain>

On Mon, 2005-06-27 at 13:56 -0700, Rich Shepard wrote:
>    Does anyone know how to change the color of the firefox vertical scroll bar
> "thumb?" It's a very light grey (as is the background of the window, but this
> is not controllable by the window manager under X) and very difficult to see
> adjacent to the white background of the application window.
> 
>    Running qtconfig also seems to have no effect.

To change to color to something specific you want, you will need to
learn XML User Interface Language (XUL), which is what Mozilla/Firefox
for the interface; the widgets are not native to your windowing
environment.  

http://www.mozilla.org/projects/xul/

Changing settings in your window manager will have no effect, except for
the window container itself.  Someone already mentioned changing themes.
You also might want to consider a browser that uses the gecko rendering
engine with a native toolkit; for GTK, my favorite options are galeon
and epiphany.

http://galeon.sourceforge.net/

http://www.gnome.org/projects/epiphany/

If you really like the Firefox feature set, there are many themes that
attempt to "native" under your preferred window manager.

Michael Chan

From mikeraz at patch.com  Sat Jul  9 09:07:36 2005
From: mikeraz at patch.com (Michael Rasmussen)
Date: Sat, 9 Jul 2005 09:07:36 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] earthlink mail handling
Message-ID: <20050709160736.GA12503@patch.com>

I've just received a request from someone subscribed to an email list.
She wants all of the email addresses on the list to put into her address book.

I used to use earthlink a while back and always had more spam than I
could handle.  She has her spam settings set to high that will allow
only those users in her address book to be delivered.  She just needs
to lower her spam settings so that all email comes in and she then
tags those users who she thinks are spam.  From experience,  I would
say lose earthlink, and lose your spam.

From heinlein at madboa.com  Sat Jul  9 23:03:24 2005
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Sat, 9 Jul 2005 23:03:24 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] TCP connect() times out
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0507092258250.29063@pecos.madboa.com>

I got an odd new problem. The host in question (Gentoo, SPARC, 2.4.30) 
is on a five-node LAN sitting behind a NAT gateway.

I can

a. ping any host on the LAN or Internet (i.e., ICMP works fine)
b. query any DNS server on LAN or Internet (UDP works fine)
c. receive TCP connections from LAN or Internet
d. initiate TCP connections to hosts on LAN

I cannot

* initiate TCP connections to hosts on the Internet

The problem isn't specific to any TCP protocol; they all fail: smtp, 
telnet, ftp, http, ...

All the other hosts on the LAN have no trouble initiating connections 
to off-site servers.

I'm working on isolating the interesting parts of strace output; right 
now the only thing that leaps out at me is that connect() is timing 
out, e.g.,

connect(3, {sa_family=AF_INET, sin_port=htons(80), 
sin_addr=inet_addr("129.95.36.100")}, 16) = -1 ETIMEDOUT (Connection 
timed out)

The problem just began in the past 24 hours. I've recently emerged 
some pam packages, but core things like glibc and the kernel haven't 
changed. Clues?

-- Paul Heinlein <heinlein at madboa.com>



From heinlein at madboa.com  Sun Jul 10 08:10:51 2005
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Sun, 10 Jul 2005 08:10:51 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Re: SOLVED: TCP connect() times out
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0507092258250.29063@pecos.madboa.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0507092258250.29063@pecos.madboa.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0507100809430.28007@pecos.madboa.com>

The problem seems to have been that tcp congestion notification 
(net.ipv4.tcp_ecn) had been turned on, which doesn't work and play 
well with my internet connection. Turning it off got things working 
again.

My guess (and it's only a guess) is that /etc/sysctl.conf was tweaked 
when I updated the Gentoo sys-apps/baselayout package back on June 10. 
When I rebooted the machine yesterday morning, it got a new ECN 
setting, which hosed things.

Well, that blew a few hours of my time. Feh. :-)

From heinlein at madboa.com  Mon Jul 11 09:33:42 2005
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Mon, 11 Jul 2005 09:33:42 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] TCP connect() times out
In-Reply-To: <55ef240d0507110905f0637bd@mail.gmail.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0507092258250.29063@pecos.madboa.com>
	<55ef240d0507110905f0637bd@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <42D29F66.1030602@madboa.com>

On 7/11/05 9:05 AM, Nick Aubert wrote:

>>I've recently emerged some pam packages
> 
> I think that's your first clue right there.  It isn't unreasonable
> that security modules should affect your network performance.  Which
> packages did you emerge?

As I mentioned in my "SOLVED" follow-up, the trouble was with the ECN 
bits in TCP headers. Non ECN-capable routing equipment is supposed to 
ignore those bits, but "there are some broken firewalls and 
load-balancers in the Internet that instead respond to an ECN-setup SYN 
packet with a reset" [RFC 3360, sect. 3].

The solution, once identified, was simple:

# sysctl -w net.ipv4.tcp_ecn=0

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

From griffint at pobox.com  Mon Jul 11 15:29:00 2005
From: griffint at pobox.com (Terry Griffin)
Date: Mon, 11 Jul 2005 15:29:00 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Checking Linux Flavor
In-Reply-To: <1121119422.42d2ecbede999@webmail.fiddlers-green.info>

If the distro is LSB compatible *and* if the LSB utilities are installed
you can do this:

    lsb_release -d

See man page lsb_release(1) for other options.

| you can do this:
|
|     lsb_release -d
|
| See man page lsb_release(1) for other options.
|
| Terry
|
| On Mon, Jul 11, 2005 at 03:03:42PM -0700,
| dan at fiddlers-green.info wrote:
|
|>All,
|>
|>Do you guys know of a standard way of checking which flavor of linux your
|>running?  I know redhat and Suse use a Redhat-release and Suse-release
file in
|>etc, but what about other flavors? What do you usually do to determine the
|>linux flavor that a script is running on?
|>
|>thanks,
|>Dan H.

It's not reliable, but you can often check /etc/issue to determine the
"flavor".  However, /etc/issue and /etc/issue.net are the "banner text"
issued to login clients (console being /etc/issue and telnet/ftp using
/etc/issue.net) and are occasionally changed so as to provide minimal
information to potential script kiddies.

Message-ID: <42D33922.4030004@axpr.net>

dan at fiddlers-green.info wrote:
> Do you guys know of a standard way of checking which flavor of linux your
> running?  I know redhat and Suse use a Redhat-release and Suse-release file in
> etc, but what about other flavors? What do you usually do to determine the
> linux flavor that a script is running on?

Others have pointed you towards a standard, but don't rely on anybody
actually adhering to the standard.

None of my boxes have the LSB utilities installed on them.

Only two of my boxes (the Gentoo ones) have the distro name in
/proc/version.

Sorry, don't think there's an easy way to do it.  FWIW on Slackware,
there's /etc/slackware-version.  On Gentoo there is /etc/gentoo-release.

Rob

devices", the first step in "Starting hardware detection...". 
Then it sits.

I did a Google search:

"Searching for info file" site:http://lists.suse.com/archive/

and found a number of entries. One comment recommended putting a floppy in the 
drive. I found a file named info.txt on the CD (by looking at it on this 
machine), and copied it onto a floppy formatted with an EXT2 file system. 
This did not appear to help.

Another comment from the Google search suggested switching to the console 
screen <Ctrl><Alt><F3> before the process hung. When I did that, I watched 
insmod run through a number of steps. The last few lines on the screen read:

--------------------------------------
insmod /modules/usb-storage.ko
Hardware probing finished.
floppy: /dev/fd0
got info from cmdline
insmod /modules/piix.ko
Ok, that seems to have worked. :-)
Looking for a SUSE Linux CD...
--------------------------------------

Any ideas on what to try next?

I'm not sure what you've already tried, so some of the following might
be redundant:

Is python installed and in the path?  (ie, type 'python' and see what it says)

If so, you should be able to type 'python yourscript' to run it.  

Another way is to see exactly where the python executable is by typing
'which python' and adding a magic comment to the top of your script of
the form:

#!/usr/bin/python

Then, assuming that the script itself is executable, you can simply cd
to the script's directory and type './yourscript'

Ali

Dr. Richard B. Shepard, President      | Author of "Quantifying Environmental
Applied Ecosystem Services, Inc. (TM)  | Impact Assessments Using Fuzzy Logic"

#!/usr/bin/python
"""
  Takes a list of candidate environmental components and creates an
  output file of all combinations of components. Assumes input list
  name of 'components.in' and produces 'components.out'.
"""
import sys

def factorial(n):
  if n == 0:
    return 1
  else:
    return n * factorial(n-1)

def klistcombs(s, k):
    """Returns list of combinations of S taken K at a time.

    S must be a list.
    K must be >= 0.
    If K > len(S), an empty list is returned.
    Else if K is 0, a singleton list containing an empty list
    is returned.
    The k-combinations are returned in lexicographic order
    of their indices.
"""
    if k < 0:
        raise ValueError, "arg k == %d not >= 0" % k
    if len(s) < k:
        return []
    if k == 0:
        return [[]]

    answer = []
    indices = range(k)
    indices[-1] = indices[-1] - 1
    while 1:
        limit = len(s) - 1
        i = k - 1
        while i >= 0 and indices[i] == limit:
            i = i - 1
            limit = limit - 1
        if i < 0:
            break
        val = indices[i]
        for i in xrange( i, k ):
            val = val + 1
            indices[i] = val
        temp = []
        for i in indices:
            temp.append( s[i] )
        answer.append( temp )
    return answer        

def combos(m, n):
  num = factorial(m)
  denom = factorial(n) * factorial(m-n)
  c = num / denom
  return c
  
if __name__ == '__main__':
  combos(m, n)
  print num, denom, c

On 7/12/05, Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:
> [rshepard at salmo ~/data/fuzzyEI-assessor/code]$ ./scoping-pairs.py 10 2
> Traceback (most recent call last):
>    File "./scoping-pairs.py", line 61, in ?
>      combos(m, n)
> NameError: name 'm' is not defined
> 
>    The source file is attached. I know it's a simple error of the head
> slapping kind.

The name m is only in scope for the duration of the combos function. 
When you call it at the bottom, you have nothing in scope called m (or
n, for that matter).

Perhaps what you wanted was for the first two arguments passed at the
command line to become m and n.  You can get command line arguments
from the sys.argv collection and assign m and n accordingly.

You don't have to declare variables in Python before assigning to
them, but you still must assign them before they may be used.

HTH,

Dylan

From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)

On 7/12/05, Evan Heidtmann <clydefrog at adnap.no-ip.com> wrote:
> I am mainly looking for recommendations on manufacturers or specific
> lines of machines. Where can I find reliable, well-built machines with
> reasonable performance?

The ThinkPad T-series is quite good.  They weigh in at around 5 lbs,
and with extended battery hit 4-5 hours on a charge.  The screen
resolution ranges from 1024x768 to 1600x1200 (the T-41p, 42p and 43p
models--giving you something like 130dpi).  There is a mid-range
screen at 1400x1050.  The processor power tops out at around 2ghz, but
you can drop 2 gigs of ram in the T-43, (not sure about the T-42).  As
a side note, the T-43p *does* exist, you just have to crawl around
lonovo's thinkpad site to actually get one in your cart, last I
checked it wasn't on the "let me build it" screen.

One area of difficulty you should be aware of in laptops is power
management.  The T-40 seems to work fine with the current state of
ACPI / APM in Linux, but I've head that the T-43 has some issues. 
(I'll be getting a T-42 shortly, hopefully it's more like the
earlier..).

--Rogan

In-Reply-To: <200507120728.36034.rsteff@comcast.net>

	<200507120728.36034.rsteff@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <42D4305F.6060900@braithwaites.net>

Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> The install booted from the CD. Is it possible for it to see the drive and 
> then lose site of the drive?

Yes, absolutely. It's the IDE version of the old SCSI ID & termination 
problems (if you don't want to know what those are, count yourself among 
the blessed).

Basically, you can have two IDE devices on a cable, one jumpered as 
Master and one jumpered as Slave (there is a whole political correctness 
brain dead argument here, but I'll spare you) and get on with the gory 
details:

* If there is only one IDE drive on the cable, you typically do not have 
any drive number (0 or 1) jumpered.

* If you have another drive, you need to check whether the other drive 
is jumpered and, if so, if it is jumpered as Master; if there is no 
jumper for the drive number, you need to jumper it (generally for 
Master) and then jumper the CD drive as the opposite.

Most, but certainly not all, IDE drives have a sticker showing the 
jumper settings. If your drive does not, I recommend looking up the 
drive make model (Google is your friend). Although you certainly can get 
the settings right by trial and error, there is much greater certainty 
of error than of trial. Or something like that.

> Earlier I had tried to use Knoppix to figure out what the network card was, 
> without opening up the box. Knoppix was unable to find a file system. I 
> wondered if this box had something special done to it to make messing with 
> the drive harder, but I was able to erase the partition tables and later 
> install RH 7.0 from an old CD set, so I don't think it's the CD or the hard 
> drive.

Phase of the moon, et cetera. Just check the jumpers and see what they are.

Good luck!

 From a very pleasant back yard in Vancouver, BC,
-Ron

#!/usr/bin/python

def factorial(n):
  if int(n == 0):
    return 1
  else:
    return n * factorial(n-1)

if __name__ == '__main__':
  import sys
  n = int(sys.argv[1])
  print n * factorial(n-1)
  
-------------- next part --------------
#!/usr/bin/python
"""
  Calculates the number of combinations of 'm' things taken 'n' at a time.
"""  
import factorial

def combos(m, n):
  num = factorial.factorial(m)
  denom = factorial.factorial(n) * factorial.factorial(m-n)
  c = num / denom
  return c

if '__name__' == '__main__':
  import sys, factorial
  m = int(sys.argv[1])
  n = int(sys.argv[2]
  num = factorial.factorial(m)
  denom = factorial.factorial(n) * factorial.factorial(m-n)
  c = num / denom
  print c

From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Tue Jul 12 21:19:49 2005
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Tue, 12 Jul 2005 21:19:49 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Canned Air for Dust Removal
In-Reply-To: <200507111624.44023.rsteff@comcast.net>
References: <200507111624.44023.rsteff@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <1121228389.14348.13.camel@denk.nakedape.cc>

On Mon, 2005-07-11 at 16:24 -0700, Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> It has been recommended that I blow the dust out of some noisy fans in my 
> computers, and that a suitable product is probably found at Radio Shack. Upon 
> purchasing same (Velocity Dust Remover Spray), I notice that it is 1,1,1,2- 
> Tetrafluorethane. Is this the sort of thing that you folks with relevant 
> experience use?

Home Depot has a $20 dual-action vacuum/blower called the TurboVac 800
that works great for this kind of thing.  You really just want to use
the blower on sensitive areas and the vacuum with the brush attachment
cleans the inside of cases, fan blades, etc.

http://tinyurl.com/8s8qm

For the fans, buy low-viscosity sewing machine oil, peel the sticker
back and put in a couple drops of oil.  The difficult part is not
putting in too much oil and getting it all over the surface where the
sticker should re-attach.  I try to make a point of blowing out and
oiling the fans anytime I have to take a box down for an extended
period.

http://www.dansdata.com/fanmaint.htm

Wil

I think tinyurl messed up on this one, or Home Depot changed its site. I get 
to Home Depot, but the items on that page are way beyond the $20 range. What 
I get when I click on that link is a display with eight home standby 
generators ranging from $7495.00 to $11,295.00, two 12' x 20' timber building 
packages, and two wine cellar cabinets!

But, regarding vacuums, I once read that one should not use vacuums with 
plastic hoses because of the static charge built up by the flow of air 
through the hose. 

> For the fans, buy low-viscosity sewing machine oil, peel the sticker
> back and put in a couple drops of oil.  The difficult part is not
> putting in too much oil and getting it all over the surface where the
> sticker should re-attach.  I try to make a point of blowing out and
> oiling the fans anytime I have to take a box down for an extended
> period.
>
> http://www.dansdata.com/fanmaint.htm

I don't know why I forgot about that idea. I used to do it on an old unit my 
wife used for years. I'll get out the 3-in-1 the next time I open the box.

is glad to be able to continue working at ESHA.

I'm working on CareWheels Canada, which is incredibly exciting.

Basically, what CareWheels does is hire people with disabilities to 
"watch over" frail Elders (and others) who want to safely live in their 
homes instead of moving to assisted living facilities. We put about 5 
motion detectors into the Elder's home to detect their movements 
associated with their Activities of Daily Living (ADLs). So if they 
don't get out of bed by 10am, when they are normally up by 8am, or if 
they go into the washroom and don't come out for 2 hours, the AI 
(Bayesian Inference) software tells their Telecare Provider there might 
be something wrong. That person then tries to contact them to see if 
they are OK and, if they can't reach them, contacts their designated 
caregiver. In addition, the Telecare Provider will call each of their 
Elders every day, just to make contact and give them an opportunity to 
talk to someone if they are lonely.

There is more to it than that, but if you want to know more, you'll have 
to come to the party and ask me directly.

At any rate, this email is going all over the world to every continent 
(except Antarctica - I used to know someone at South Pole Station, but 
he recovered from his craziness and got a "normal" job).

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Wed Jul 13 06:28:51 2005
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Wed, 13 Jul 2005 06:28:51 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] in.comsat Explanation
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0507130621120.25867@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

   I'd greatly appreciate having one of you expert SysAdmins explain the
"service in.comsat" line in secure-log. I see this every day.

  --------------------- Connections (secure-log) Begin ------------------------

Refused Connections:
    Service in.comsat:
       localhost.localdomain (127.0.0.1): 210 Time(s)

  ---------------------- Connections (secure-log) End -------------------------

  According to the in.comsat(8) man page,

"Comsat is the server process which receives reports of incoming mail and
notifies users if they have requested this service. Comsat receives messages
on a datagram port associated with the `biff'' service specification (see
services(5) and inetd(8)). The one line messages are of the form:

     user at mailbox-offset

"If the user specified is logged in to the system and the associated terminal
has the owner execute bit turned on (by a `biff y '' the offset is used as a
seek offset into the appropriate mailbox file and the first 7 lines or 560
characters of the message are printed on the user's terminal. Lines which
appear to be part of the message header other than the `From '' or `Subject''
lines are not included in the output."

   I don't know why it's turned on, how to turn it off, or why it's there in
the first place. The man page is not that comprehensive. More to the point,
should I even care about it?

TIA for the education,

Rich

In-Reply-To: <200507122017.01345.rsteff@comcast.net>
References: <200507121749.45357.rsteff@comcast.net>
	<42D46BB3.6080607@easystreet.com>

Fraudulent SPAM - 

Since the government seems to do nothing besides collecting statistics,
you may wish to consider the equally futile but more entertaining option
of "mugu-baiting".  Mugu is the Nigerian word for spam.  So mugu-baiting
is the hobby of contacting these criminals under a pseudonym, winding
them up, inducing them to waste a lot of time and money, etc. 

There are numerous websites dedicated to this activity, such as
baita.mugu.co.uk, 419eater.com, etc.   Some of the exploits are hilarious.

Keith

Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

Subject: [PLUG] Beginning Python Question #7
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0507130847470.25867@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

   I found a function that accepts input of a list of items (s) and an integer
(k), and produces another list of all combinations of s things taken k at a
time. The initial code is this:

def klistcombs(s, k):
     """Returns list of combinations of S taken K at a time.

     S must be a list.
     K must be >= 0.
     If K > len(S), an empty list is returned.
     Else if K is 0, a singleton list containing an empty list
     is returned.
     The k-combinations are returned in lexicographic order
     of their indices.
"""
     output = open('components.out', 'w')
     input = open('components.in', 'r')
     s = input.read()

   I added the file statements because I want to use a text file on disk as
the source of the input list. However, it's not working.

   When I run interactively, I get an illegal syntax error at the final 'n'
when I specify:

>>> klistcombs(components.in, 2)
   File "<stdin>", line 1
     klistcombs(components.in, 2)
                            ^
SyntaxError: invalid syntax

   If I change the name of the file so there is no extension, the error
changes to:

>>> klistcombs(components, 2)
Traceback (most recent call last):
   File "<stdin>", line 1, in ?
NameError: name 'components' is not defined

   Since I cannot get past invoking the function, I assume the error is in the
way the function is defined. How can I define klistcombs() so that it will
look for the input list externally, on the hard drive?

Thanks,

Rich

Reading the KMail manual I find:

-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Message->Forward...

Forwards themessage to a new recipient. Using Inline, the message's text and 
some important header fields will be copied to the body of the new message 
wit a text marking the forwarded part. Attachments will be forwarded as 
attachments of the new message. Using As Attachment the message and its 
attachments will become an attachment of the new message. The original 
messsage headers will be included in the forwarded message, too. Redirect 
works like forward, except that the message stays the same (even the From: 
field). The user who redirected the message is added in special header fields 
(Redirect-From, Redirect-Date, Redirect-To, etc.).
-------------------------------------------------------------------------------

> > But, regarding vacuums, I once read that one should not use vacuums with

I guess if static on a blower were a problem, then it would be a problem with 
canned air coming out the plastic tube, too. I wonder if there is something 
different about vacuums. I know that static is a known problem in woodworking 
shop vacuums that use PVC pipe and that grounding straps are used to deal 
with it. Apparently there have been some cases of wood dust exploding that 
brought this to folks attention.

particularly concerned about the ability to programically access the
camera (well, and resolution, but that's something I don't feel
completely ignorant about).  Next on the list of concerns is driver
support... are webcams prettymuch plug&play with linux these days?

Thanks,
--Rogan

> > original headers, I don't find them. I tried Forward->Inline, and
> > Forward->Redirect, too, but did not get the original headers in those,
> > either.
>
> The sure-fire way is View -> All headers, then copy it into a text file.
> Then attach the file and send it away.

Well, that makes sense. I guess it's too simple to be obvious. Thanks for the 
idea. I'll do that until I convert to Thunderbird. I've been happy with KMail 
for the most part, but a couple of limitations have turned up, like this 
forward as attachment thing (which I've discovered Thunderbird does 
correctly), and the limited organizational possibilities of KMail's address 
book. I've only had Thunderbird on the machine for a couple of weeks, and 
have only used it once or twice.

BTW, this is KMail 1.6.2 (Using KDE 3.2.1) which installs with SuSE 9.1, 
should someone look at this in the future and have different results on their 
own version.

Subject: [PLUG] USB camera (webcam?) recommendations
In-Reply-To: <c834ec805071312597b303f77@mail.gmail.com>

On Wednesday 13 July 2005 12:59 pm, Rogan Creswick wrote:

> Do any of you have any input on what to look for in one of these?  I'm
> particularly concerned about the ability to programically access the
> camera (well, and resolution, but that's something I don't feel
> completely ignorant about).  Next on the list of concerns is driver
> support... are webcams prettymuch plug&play with linux these days?

As you look for the right camera, don't rule out still photography digital 
cameras. Some of them can be used as USB cameras.

I haven't tried it yet, but the manual for my Canon Digital Rebel (first 
version) says that it can be viewed via a computer when connected to the USB 
port, and that some of the controls may also be manipulated. I haven't looked 
into what Linux software supports the feature, either.

It's advantage is the selection of lenses.

Its disadvantage is that it is a good bit bigger than simple USB cameras.

The Drupal project (drupal.org) produces a good CMS package on almost
nothing. Up until this weekend, when they solicited donations for a new
server, AFAIK there was no money whatsoever on hand. Now they have
around $10k plus Summer Of Code money, which might be as much as $5500
at the end of the summer. A non-profit entity is in the works but all
money is currently held by the head developer.

It's not on the same scale as Debian, but it might fit on your list.

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Wed Jul 13 19:41:05 2005
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Wed, 13 Jul 2005 19:41:05 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Hello from Maryland, Speakers Bureau
Message-ID: <20050714024105.GA2873@gate.kl-ic.com>


I'm visiting my wife's family near Baltimore.  I noticed that the
Columbia area Linux Users Group was meeting, and that they did not
have a speaker.  So I volunteered, and gave my Dirvish talk.  It was
well received, about 20 people in attendance.

A lot of LUG's find themselves without speakers, but I bet that there
are many of us with prepared talks scattered around the country; with
OSDL in the area, we probably get speakers visiting from all over.
If we could connect potential visiting speakers with PLUG, I bet we
could make Dave's and Allan's job a lot easier, and our meetings more
interesting.

Perhaps there is somebody who can set up a "Linux Speaker's Bureau".
User groups that host speakers could put their meeting times and
planned schedules up, while potential speakers (dozens from PLUG
alone) could check their travel plans against the open slots and
volunteer if they wish.  It would be a good way for the speakers to
connect up with the locals and learn about Cool Stuff in the area.

Any interest?

Keith

Even without it being official, you need proof of citizenship. That's 
what both the US and Canadian border guards told me. An Oregon drivers 
license is NOT proof of citizenship. It's only proof that you have been 
given permission to operate a vehicle. A social security card is NOT 
proof of citizenship. It's only proof that the US government has a 
number assigned to you for the purpose of taxing you.

According to what I was told by immigrations, the best documentation is 
either your birth certificate or a passport.
