>>>>> "Jeme" == Jeme A Brelin <jeme at brelin.net> writes:

Jeme> The City of Portland is going to build a city-wide wireless network of
Jeme> undetermined freedom and openness.  However this is constructed,
Jeme> private agents will still have the option of offering wireless
Jeme> services (overlapping coverage happens all the time), so the public
Jeme> service will not prohibit "better" services offered by the private
Jeme> sector.

How naive you are, sir.

The economics of providing a private net once a "free" service is
offered subsidized by public funds won't let that fly.

Imagine me trying to construct a private toll road that paralleled
Sunset Highway.  Even if I could get approval from the city, why would
people *pay* to take my road when Sunset Highway already exists?

I'd have to offer something that was *clearly* a premium, and that's
just not going to be economically feasible.

So, effectively, using tax dollars to subsidize what is likely to be
an inferior net (because along with tax dollars comes the republican
agenda) will lock out the opportunity to get a *real* net driven by
market pressures.

Hence, this is a bad idea.  *All* public-works projects for "city wide"
nets are a bad idea.  At least, in a republican-driven world.

On Jul 27, 2005, at 11:27 AM, Randal L. Schwartz wrote:
>>>>>> "Randall" == Randall Lucas <rlucas at tercent.com> writes:
> Randall> There will definitely be politicos and commercial vendors
> Randall> there.  It would be outstanding if we could get some strong
> Randall> representation from the F/OSS and Linux communities there as
> Randall> well.
> You mean so we can be there to protest the use of public funds to
> create a virtual monopoly against which private industry cannot offer
> alternatives?  Cool!

Uhm.....How would it be even *possible* to create a virtual monopoly, 
on wireless access, to which the private industry *cannot* offer 
alternatives?

Seriously, I don't see how this would even be technically possible. 
Even typical monopoly things like, oh, drinking water and electricity 
and phone services now have numerous *private* alternatives. Sure, the 
public utility drinking water is cheap, but "distilled water" sells 
like gangbusters, and if you don't like the power grid monopolies, you 
can go solar or use other generators, and voice services has 
cell/VoiP/skype/etc...

> No more monopolies.  Microsoft, government, anyone.  Free the
> airwaves.

WiFi uses free spectrum... er... and I'm pretty sure the City of 
Portland can't overrule the FCC.

> If the government's "net" chooses to censor connections to
> "unapproved" sites or services, or implements poor intrusive web
> proxying and filtering,, we'll never get a chance to have a third
> party provide an alternative.

Again, why not?

Heck, that situation has "incentives for private industry alternative" 
written all over it. I can already imagine the slogans: "Tired of your 
free public WiFi coverage sucking? For only 25 bucks a month, you can 
have citywide WiFi that doesn't suck!"

> And you can bet that the
> right wingers will want to make sure public airwaves aren't used to
> download "porn" or "abortion" information.  End of freedom.

"Tired of using a public WiFi network that filters out your favorite 
websites? Get citywide WiFi that doesn't suck for only 25 bucks a 
month!"

> If there is to be a citywide net, the market will provide it.  No need
> to use public funds.

Well, if that was true, the market *would* have provided it already, 
no? However, it seems that there was no need to provide it yet, as the 
market was quite happy to keep us locked into more expensive 
technologies (such as cell modems) that don't require larger 
infrastructure investments. As soon as there is market pressure from a 
public utility, private industry has an incentive to invest in building 
additional infrastructure in order to capture that market segment from 
the public sector, and offer a "better" product than an existing public 
service provides...

Think about "free" TV stations vs. cable. "Free" drinking fountains vs. 
bottled water. "Free" parks vs. private country clubs. "Free" public 
concerts vs. private concerts. "Free" downtown public transportation 
vs. cars. In all these cases, (and I'm sure many more), private 
industry offered alternatives, even though there was an existing 
"free/cheap" service, subsidized by the public. In some cases, private 
industry alternatives were actually *created* by the public investment.

> I presume this is why you want us there.
>
> Or were you intending some other common voice?

Common voice? In F/OSS? LOL!

engineering and maintenance tasks.  Of course it will mean a larger staff, 
but only minor incremental management increase.

I would rather see the city diverting public funds directly to projects 
like the PTP Mississippi Avenue unwiring and supplying NNIs into existing 
city internetworking, but the structures just aren't in place at the 
moment to support community-based services.

From rlucas at tercent.com  Wed Jul 27 19:26:21 2005
From: rlucas at tercent.com (Randall Lucas)
Date: Wed, 27 Jul 2005 19:26:21 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Re: Unwire Portland public meeting tomorrow
Message-ID: <42E8424D.3090400@tercent.com>

> The economics of providing a private net once a "free" service is
> offered subsidized by public funds won't let that fly.

Quick reality check: the network deal is going to get made, PLUG mailing
list exchanges or no.  Our opportunity to help steer it towards the good
is by showing up to this meeting, learning about it, and lobbying our
public officials.

The only "free" service under discussion is the quid pro quo to the
public for using its existing assets (light poles, etc.).  Rest assured,
commercially viable connection speeds on the new net will cost you a
pretty penny.

Right now, though, the "free" service is going to be a fat pipe to the
walled garden.  A skinny (e.g. ~ 10 kpbs) pipe to the unrestricted
Internet is what I'd like to see, instead.

> Imagine me trying to construct a private toll road that paralleled
> Sunset Highway.  Even if I could get approval from the city, why would
> people *pay* to take my road when Sunset Highway already exists?

Actually, if we had a hope in hell of getting eminent domain rights to
the necessary land, I'd join you on that project in a minute!  Imagine
who /wouldn't/ pay $2 to be able to go 60 mph to Hillsboro at 5:15 PM? ;)

Best,

Randall

On Jul 27, 2005, at 6:06 PM, Randal L. Schwartz wrote:
>>>>>> "Jeme" == Jeme A Brelin <jeme at brelin.net> writes:
> Jeme> The City of Portland is going to build a city-wide wireless 
> network of
> Jeme> undetermined freedom and openness.  However this is constructed,
> Jeme> private agents will still have the option of offering wireless
> Jeme> services (overlapping coverage happens all the time), so the 
> public
> Jeme> service will not prohibit "better" services offered by the 
> private
> Jeme> sector.
> How naive you are, sir.
> The economics of providing a private net once a "free" service is
> offered subsidized by public funds won't let that fly.
> Imagine me trying to construct a private toll road that paralleled
> Sunset Highway.  Even if I could get approval from the city, why would
> people *pay* to take my road when Sunset Highway already exists?

Hm.....How about: Faster travel times. Better maintained road surface. 
Less congestion. Less police presence/harassment. Better roadside 
stops. Dedicated emergency services access roads.

> I'd have to offer something that was *clearly* a premium, and that's
> just not going to be economically feasible.

Why not? Judging from the history of most other public services, there 
are private counterparts created that are economically feasible, and 
which offer clear premium services. Free public schooling doesn't mean 
that private schools don't exist. Free public policing doesn't mean 
that there aren't security firms and private bodyguards.

> So, effectively, using tax dollars to subsidize what is likely to be
> an inferior net (because along with tax dollars comes the republican
> agenda) will lock out the opportunity to get a *real* net driven by
> market pressures.

The "inferior net" (I'm not touching the political swipe).... which 
people would then gladly upgrade *from*, in order to get *to* having a 
superior product. You've already noted *why* the market could offer 
profitable alternatives.

The market can then fight over which of their new products are more 
superior, instead of doing what the market is currently doing (which 
doesn't seem to be an awful lot, save for cell connections)...

Let's see, in this case, they (private sector) could then offer better 
citywide area coverage, faster access points, better security 
standards, in-line IPS, better tech support, more overall uptime, less 
fear of "government snooping", no "censorship"... and that's just a few 
seconds worth of ideas. Who knows, maybe there are numerous premiums 
that the market hasn't thought of yet, because the market hasn't 
invested themselves in actually creating the *product* yet.

- -Ronabop

AthlonRob wrote:
> Randal L. Schwartz wrote:
> 
>>If there is to be a citywide net, the market will provide it.  No need
>>to use public funds.
> 
> 
> Amen.  This is not Communist Russia, we *do* have private industry.

Yup. And that's why I pay US$900 a month for health coverage here and 
will pay US$80 up in Canada. And US$1250 per year for my motorcycle 
(with NO accidents) and will pay about US$100 up there.

Allowing private industry to take on a monopoly position is why taxes 
are SO much higher here than they are there - the difference is that I 
have to pay taxes to corporations whose primary interest is profit here 
(ever consider driving without insurance?), while there I get to pay 
taxes to the guvmint.

When our version of State Monopoly Capitalism (described so well by Karl 
Marx about 120 years ago) is compared with a mixed economy like 
Canada's, it always comes out looking pretty lame. So we have folks like 
Gush Windbag telling us, "But that's SOCIALISM, folks!" And I answer:

"Alright! Give me more socialism!"

From merlyn at stonehenge.com  Thu Jul 28 11:54:44 2005
From: merlyn at stonehenge.com (Randal L. Schwartz)
Date: 28 Jul 2005 11:54:44 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Re: MySQL Statement?

>>>>> "Steven" == Steven D Coffman <s.coffman at tradetrans.net> writes:

Steven> I need to clean up our database. The tables and files are a bit
Steven> complicated but the general gist of what I need to do is like this
Steven> (sorry.. best way I can explain it is psuedo code)

Steven> Select Atable where Company = 1212 and Date < "2005-01-01"
Steven>   ** ok, this is the basic select I want from the file and I only need
Steven> the "fileID" from this table **

Steven> *** Once all of the fileID's from the above select has been performed, I
Steven> want to run a delete routine for every fileID from that first select ***
Steven> For (?? Every fileID)
Steven>   Delete tableB, Delete tableC....etc.
Steven>   Next fileID

With a *real* database (hint: "not MySQL"), referential cascading delete
would take care of that.  Short of that, you could do something like:

DELETE
FROM tableb
WHERE fileid IN (
  SELECT fileid
  FROM tablea
  WHERE company = 1212 AND date < "2005-01-01"
)

Presuming MySQL now supports subselects (which I think it has, finally
catching it up with the 90s).  Repeat this as needed for each of your
other tables.

Of course, friends don't let friends use MySQL any more.

From heinlein at madboa.com  Thu Jul 28 13:04:00 2005
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Thu, 28 Jul 2005 13:04:00 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Re: MySQL Statement?
In-Reply-To: <000b01c593ab$92dffa50$3c01a8c0@mango>
References: <000b01c593ab$92dffa50$3c01a8c0@mango>
Message-ID: <42E93A30.4010005@madboa.com>

On 7/28/05 12:35 PM, Steven D. Coffman wrote:
> Thanks guys,
> Yes, I've noticed some problems using MySQL, lol, plus we have an older
> version... so I'm just selecting the first part and placing it to a
> comma delimited file, then pasting them in for the delete statements..
> guess I'll be here all day!

Get mysql to build the statements for you, e.g.,


echo <<ENDQUERY | mysql --batch yourDatabase | sed 's!\t! !g'
select
   'delete from tableB where fileID =', fileID, ';',
   'delete from tableC where fileID =', fileID, ';',
   'delete from tableD where fileID =', fileID, ';',
   'delete from tableE where fileID =', fileID, ';'
from
   tableA
where
   tableA.Company = 1212 and tableA.Date < '2005-01-01';
ENDQUERY


Pipe the result to a file, edit as necessary, and execute the resulting 
instruction set as an SQL query.

Note that not all versions of sed will do the right thing here, but GNU 
sed works as expected.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

Randal L. Schwartz wrote:
> With a *real* database (hint: "not MySQL"), referential cascading delete
> would take care of that.  Short of that, you could do something like:

How real does a database need to be before you consider it a real
database?  ;-)

> DELETE
> FROM tableb
> WHERE fileid IN (
>   SELECT fileid
>   FROM tablea
>   WHERE company = 1212 AND date < "2005-01-01"
> )
> 
> Presuming MySQL now supports subselects (which I think it has, finally
> catching it up with the 90s).  Repeat this as needed for each of your
> other tables.

That's a perfect example of why MySQL hasn't supported subqueries for so
long.  People who don't know what they're doing over-use them when joins
make far more sense.

How about something like:

DELETE tableb
FROM tableb
LEFT JOIN tablea
ON tableb.fileid = tableb.fileid
WHERE tablea.company = 1212 AND tablea.date < "2005-01-01"

That's what my one year of SQL experience (that is to say, very little)
tells me to use.  Warning: it is untested.  :-)

Rob

Rich Shepard wrote:
>   It's been a while since I re-did my web site, but ... elements have
> padding, margins, and something else which I forget off-hand. The
> padding is
> between the content and the box, the margin and other thing are outside.

> look it up tomorrow in my Eric Meyer on CSS books if you'd like me to do

The CSS Box Model.  They have padding, borders, and margins.  Padding
goes between the block elements and the border, the border around the
padding, and the margin around the border.  The W3C page on the box
model does a quite nice job of explaining it (oddly enough)...

http://www.w3.org/TR/CSS21/box.html

Rob


From merlyn at stonehenge.com  Thu Jul 28 20:31:29 2005

>>>>> "AthlonRob" == AthlonRob  <athlonrob at axpr.net> writes:

AthlonRob> How about something like:

AthlonRob> DELETE tableb
AthlonRob> FROM tableb
AthlonRob> LEFT JOIN tablea
AthlonRob> ON tableb.fileid = tableb.fileid
AthlonRob> WHERE tablea.company = 1212 AND tablea.date < "2005-01-01"

You can't do that.  That's why I did it my way.  The SQL99 syntax is:

DELETE FROM table WHERE condition

You don't get to spell out a join there.  In your join, would you
be deleting rows from tableb or tablea?  See why that doesn't work!

From athlonrob at axpr.net  Thu Jul 28 20:42:11 2005
From: athlonrob at axpr.net (AthlonRob)
Date: Thu, 28 Jul 2005 20:42:11 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Re: MySQL Statement?
In-Reply-To: <86pst2b84u.fsf@blue.stonehenge.com>
References: <86sly0gc87.fsf@blue.stonehenge.com>	<000501c5939b$8c00be00$3c01a8c0@mango>	<86br4mep6z.fsf@blue.stonehenge.com>
	<42E97C8C.60004@axpr.net> <86pst2b84u.fsf@blue.stonehenge.com>
Message-ID: <42E9A593.5050000@axpr.net>

Randal L. Schwartz wrote:
>>>>>>"AthlonRob" == AthlonRob  <athlonrob at axpr.net> writes:
> AthlonRob> DELETE tableb
> AthlonRob> FROM tableb
> AthlonRob> LEFT JOIN tablea
> AthlonRob> ON tableb.fileid = tableb.fileid
> AthlonRob> WHERE tablea.company = 1212 AND tablea.date < "2005-01-01"
> 
> You can't do that.  That's why I did it my way.  The SQL99 syntax is:

Oh, but you can.  See http://dev.mysql.com/doc/mysql/en/delete.html for
more information.  Or give it a try.  I'll bet it's quicker than your
subquery, as well.

> DELETE FROM table WHERE condition

It may not be valid SQL99, but it has been supported in MySQL since
verison 4.0.0.  You'd have to ask a MySQL guru about it's propriety.
I'm far from a MySQL guru.

> You don't get to spell out a join there.  In your join, would you
> be deleting rows from tableb or tablea?  See why that doesn't work!

Look at the top line of the above query and you'll see I'm deleting rows
from tableb.  In real life, I generally spell it out as tableb.* so it
is more clear which table is having rows removed from it.  Note you
could remove the rows from both tables by listing both the table names
in the DELETE line above (tableb, tablea).

IMHO, you shouldn't badmouth a piece of software as big and popular as
MySQL without really understanding said software and its deficiencies.
Yes, it is far from perfect, but a lot of what people say "sucks" about
MySQL doesn't really suck at all these days.

This ain't your father's MySQL.

Rob


From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Thu Jul 28 20:43:35 2005

   If I want a directory on a web server to be invisible to the public, but

   I know the read and execute bits let people list the directory contents

removing of files. So I'm checking.

   Perhaps the better way is to put the files in a directory on the server but
not have any links to that directory. This means users must know it's there
in order to access files in it.

   Are there simple username/passwd setups that will also control access to
directories and files that are easy to set up and for a non-techie to
maintain?

TIA,

Rich

On 7/28/05, Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:
>    If I want a directory on a web server to be invisible to the public, but
> allow individuals to download files by specifying them by name, do I set the
> directory permissions at 700?

Just make the directory non-searchable. (chmod o-x <directory>) and
you should be set.  I'm sure there's a better way to do what you want,
with some "real" authentication, as you inticate below, but I think
this does what you describe above.  The user and group bits shouldn't
matter. (unless, perhaps if the file is owned by the same user that
the webserver runs as... but that's just conjecture..)

From grishnav at egosurf.net  Thu Jul 28 22:51:22 2005
From: grishnav at egosurf.net (Grishnav)
Date: Thu, 28 Jul 2005 22:51:22 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Directory Permissions
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0507282038450.16770@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0507282038450.16770@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <42E9C3DA.5040800@egosurf.net>

Rich Shepard wrote:

>   If I want a directory on a web server to be invisible to the public, 
> but
> allow individuals to download files by specifying them by name, do I 
> set the
> directory permissions at 700?


How are they going to be downloading them? Via ftp/sftp/etc. or via the 
web server?

In the case of Apache, what you want to do is turn of indexing for the 
directory:


<Directory /foo/bar/bah>
    Options -Indexes
</Directory>

The Unix permissions on the directory don't matter so much as long as 
Apache can read the directory and the files and they can't be 
overwritten by anyone else...

From athlonrob at axpr.net  Fri Jul 29 05:35:44 2005
From: athlonrob at axpr.net (AthlonRob)
Date: Fri, 29 Jul 2005 05:35:44 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Re: MySQL Statement?
In-Reply-To: <8664utc282.fsf@blue.stonehenge.com>
References: <86sly0gc87.fsf@blue.stonehenge.com>	<000501c5939b$8c00be00$3c01a8c0@mango>	<86br4mep6z.fsf@blue.stonehenge.com>
	<42E97C8C.60004@axpr.net>	<86pst2b84u.fsf@blue.stonehenge.com>
	<42E9A593.5050000@axpr.net> <8664utc282.fsf@blue.stonehenge.com>
Message-ID: <42EA22A0.1060400@axpr.net>

Randal L. Schwartz wrote:
> It's still not ACID compliant when data gets lost, even with InnoDB
> tables.  As long as you can store 1e20 into an int field, only to have
> it silently truncated to 65535, it's not going into any production use
> that *I* have for it.

Again, I'm not a MySQL guru, so I spoke with a few folks who are.
Everybody seems to be in agreement that if you're using InnoDB tables,
you're fully ACID compliant.  Now, I understand there is some difference
in interpretations for your above truncation case.  Some believe such
things have nothing to do with ACID, some believe the database should at
least warn you before the truncation, and some believe it should
outright refuse any query that tries to put a too-large number in to an
int field that isn't set up to hold said number.

Why you would be putting a huge number in to a field that isn't set up
to hold a huge number seems like an important question, as well.

But, fear not.  With MySQL 4.1 (the current recommended release) you can
configurably have warnings pop up for such queries.  In MySQL 5.0, you
can have warnings appear for such queries or have it outright refuse a
query that puts a too-large number in to an int field.  Yay for choice!

> AthlonRob> This ain't your father's MySQL.
> 
> Yeah, it's my confused uncle's MySQL. :)
> 
> PostgreSQL.  It just works.

MySQL.  It just works, too.  ;-)

Really, it's great to have choice out there... it keeps things
innovative.  Unfortunately there's a lot of MySQL-related FUD out there
mostly based around old issues that haven't existed for years.  Yes,
MySQL didn't support transactions and subqueries and such five years
ago.  But it does today... and it does a nice job of it today.

Rob

From clydefrog at adnap.no-ip.com  Fri Jul 29 09:39:23 2005
From: clydefrog at adnap.no-ip.com (Evan Heidtmann)
Date: Fri, 29 Jul 2005 09:39:23 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Thinkpad T42, Debian sarge, and the MBR
Message-ID: <1122655163.2669.15.camel@localhost.localdomain>

My thinkpad was delivered yesterday morning by a friendly man in a brown
suit. I promptly installed Debian and put GRUB on the MBR. Dual boot now
works well, but I cannot get into "IBM Rescue and Recovery" by pressing
the "Access IBM" button during POST. Before the install, I could.

After discovering this, but before doing anything more, I booted to
windows and burned the "IBM Product Recovery" CDs (7 CDs in total!)

It appears from reading other people's stories that the problem with
booting IBM's special recovery application lies in replacing the
factory-installed MBR with GRUB's MBR. 

First question: does the netinst r0a debian-installer make a backup of
the MBR? If so, how do I restore it?

Second question: Are those CDs I made likely to restore to factory
condition, or might they fail partway through and leave me with no
windows, no special partition, and no Debian?

Thanks for your help,
Evan

From eli.stair at gmail.com  Fri Jul 29 12:15:29 2005
From: eli.stair at gmail.com (Eli Stair)
Date: Fri, 29 Jul 2005 13:15:29 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] Logrorate: checking whether a file is open with prerorate?
Message-ID: <f4468cae05072912157f7a02b8@mail.gmail.com>

I've got a simple problem that I don't want to come up with a complex
solution for; I see the complex solution as writing my own logrorate
if I can't figure out how to do the simple thing :)

Problem:

I've gotta support a bad-bad-bad application that doesn't simply
append data to its log, it keeps the file open in memory, and writes
to it "from the last known offset into the file, according to its last
write"... according the the brilliant developer.

The issue is that I cannot use the create (move and create new
logfile) OR truncatecompress options... can't move it because it's
open for writing constantly (canNOT bring down the app), and when the
truncatecompress option is used, on the next write by the daemon the
"OLD" data that was removed becomes zero bytes up to the next write
offset:

The file becomes a gargantuan sparse file with some text at the end. 
At least they compress well :)

Solution:

use the prerotate script option to check each file being operated on,
and make sure it's not open; if it IS just do not operate on it (exit
and do nothing).

Unfortunately, even though I can have logrotate run the prerotate
script for EACH file matched by the wildcard (nosharedscripts
directive), there doesn't seem to be a documented way to know WHAT
file is being operated on per iteration.

Has anyone else had a similar situation, and thus a solution (any
solution) other than writing a cusom backup script?

Cheers,

/eli

-- 
--I'm on vacation, and so is my .sig!


From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Fri Jul 29 12:24:45 2005
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Fri, 29 Jul 2005 12:24:45 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Wireless PCMCIA cards
In-Reply-To: <20050728233147.B20897@defiant.ncplus.net>
References: <20050728233147.B20897@defiant.ncplus.net>
Message-ID: <1122665085.3385.106.camel@eel.myhealthbank.com>

On Thu, 2005-07-28 at 23:31 -0700, Bruce Kingsland wrote:
> I know that some of you have working wireless in your laptops. I want
> to know what cards you have and what driver, and how you got it
> working. Hopefully that will result in me having a working solution
> sooner!

I bought a really cheap Gateway-branded card ($5+shipping) on eBay that
turned out to be a PrismII chipset but unrecognized vendor IDs.  Found
the IDs, put them in the config file and it's worked fine since.  I
submitted a bug on the pcmcia-cs site to have the vendor IDs recognized
in the default configs; if you get one and don't have the latest (or
there hasn't been a new release since I submitted the bug), it's three
lines of configuration.

Wil

Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc>
Naked Ape Consulting, Ltd. <http://nakedape.cc>

From eli.stair at gmail.com  Fri Jul 29 14:27:38 2005
From: eli.stair at gmail.com (Eli Stair)
Date: Fri, 29 Jul 2005 15:27:38 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] Logrotate: checking whether a file is open with prerotate?
Message-ID: <f4468cae050729142749a49c60@mail.gmail.com>

I've got a simple problem that I don't want to come up with a complex
solution for; I see the complex solution as writing my own logrorate
if I can't figure out how to do the simple thing :)

Problem:

I've gotta support a bad-bad-bad application that doesn't simply
append data to its log, it keeps the file open in memory, and writes
to it "from the last known offset into the file, according to its last
write"... according the the brilliant developer.

The issue is that I cannot use the create (move and create new
logfile) OR truncatecompress options... can't move it because it's
open for writing constantly (canNOT bring down the app), and when the
truncatecompress option is used, on the next write by the daemon the
"OLD" data that was removed becomes zero bytes up to the next write
offset:

The file becomes a gargantuan sparse file with some text at the end.
At least they compress well :)

Solution:

use the prerotate script option to check each file being operated on,
and make sure it's not open; if it IS just do not operate on it (exit
and do nothing).

Unfortunately, even though I can have logrotate run the prerotate
script for EACH file matched by the wildcard (nosharedscripts
directive), there doesn't seem to be a documented way to know WHAT
file is being operated on per iteration.

Has anyone else had a similar situation, and thus a solution (any
solution) other than writing a cusom backup script?

Cheers,

/eli

> !important; }
> 
> body#mail-google-com #ap { background: #88f !important; }
>

> body#mail-google-com #ad { font-size: x-small !important; background:
> #0f0 !important; }
> 
> 
> Coloring the different bits lets me see that, indeed, the rhs is
> narrower than it used to be.  Unfortunately, it's still located in the
> same position as it was, with a wide margin between its right edge and

Upon further investigation (ie, I found how to display more

since the last time I did any.  But I can put it on my list of things

13th century music).

In the meantime, I've come up with a kludge.  Since my main issue is

happens when displaying 80-character wide text in a less than

There's still a blank space to the right of the rhs, but I can simply ignore it.

> approximately 80 characters wide (in my preferred font), thusly:

You could:
  body#mail-google-com .mb { width: 82em; }

1 em is the size of a character.[1]

With that definition you can also increase and decrease your font size
and still keep the body window slightly wider than the 80 characters 
you desire.

[1] simplified explanation

On Fri, 29 Jul 2005, Ali Corbin wrote:

> Upon further investigation (ie, I found how to display more information
> with webdeveloper) I found that the layout was happening through a
> combination of div's and span's. Now these are new in html since the last
> time I did any. But I can put it on my list of things to learn (after
> working on my Google maps app and transcribing some 13th century music).

Ali,

   "div" is a convenient way of chunking items you want to treat as a unit; 
for example, a set of links that's separate from the body text. "span" lets
you define a string over which to apply a set of attributes.

   On my new site I use span to make the company name display differently from
the rest of the text:

<span class="aesi">Applied Ecosystem newstuff.html:Services, Inc.</span>

   The site.css for this is:

.aesi   {font-variant: small-caps;}

   Here's an example of a div within my site.css:

div#navlist {
         position: absolute;
         z-index: 100;
         top: 10em;
         left: 22%;
         width: 10em;
         background: #096;
         font-size: 125%;
         }

HTH,

Rich

On Jul 29, 2005, at 6:27 PM, Wil Cooley wrote:
> On Fri, 2005-07-29 at 18:16 -0700, Rich Shepard wrote:
>> On Fri, 29 Jul 2005, Michael Rasmussen wrote:
>>> 1 em is the size of a character.[1]
>>    It's actually the size of an 'em-dash' which is entered in TeX as 
>> '--'.
> Actually, and em-dash is a dash that's the width of an 'M', as Ali just
> mentioned, not the other way around.

<pedantic level="excessive">

That's a popular way to think about it (em and en dashes being 'M' and 
'N' width in a given font), however, that's not entirely the whole 
story... some fonts have no letter 'M' or 'N', and thus, the meaning 
has changed over the years. An em-dash is now typically one "em" unit 
in length, regardless of the size (or presence) of the letter 'M' in a 
given font. An en is half of an em.

So, if an em unit is not actually fixed to an 'M' character, what 
determines it's size? It's fixed to the size of an imaginary box which 
all characters in a given font can fit into. So if a font had a larger 
letter in it than a letter 'M', the em unit for that font would be the 
size of the largest letter, horizontally as well as vertically (as not 
all character systems are horizontal).

See:
<http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Em_%28typography%29>
<http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dash>

Of course, for stylistic reasons, typographers do create fonts where an 
em-dash is *not* the size of the em, and the en-dash is *not* the size 
of an en or even half of an en, but the em size is constant, with an en 
being half of an em.

</pedantic>

I'm sure you were all dying to know that.

-Bop
--
Ronin Professional Consulting LLC
4245 NE Alberta Ct.
Portland, OR 97218
678-522-1322/503-282-1370

that sometimes show up here just don't cut it in a proportional font.)

Ali


From holger001 at frogleg.net  Sat Jul 30 18:45:47 2005
From: holger001 at frogleg.net (Holger Stephan)

Subject: [PLUG] Internet Gateway for Hotel
Message-ID: <42EC2D4B.1090303@frogleg.net>

Funseekers,

To provide controlled and metered Internet connectivity at a hotel (up 
to 200 people), does any of you have a pointer where to find either a 
reasonably priced, programmable router or a Linux router package for a 
DIY box? It should somehow work together with DHCP: if a guest pays for 
Internet (s)he should receive an IP address and timed access to the 
Internet.

If DIY is the way to go and someone is interested to do the iptable 
juggling on this project (not secured yet), please holler. I'll do the 
(Java -) application with web based interface. I'd mostly need shell 
scripts (or anything executable) to turn on/off routing for an 
individual IP address.

Thanks!!

Holger


From aaron at bavariati.org  Sat Jul 30 19:23:31 2005
From: aaron at bavariati.org (Aaron Burt)
Date: Sat, 30 Jul 2005 19:23:31 -0700

On Sat, Jul 30, 2005 at 06:45:47PM -0700, Holger Stephan wrote:
> Funseekers,
> 
> To provide controlled and metered Internet connectivity at a hotel (up 
> to 200 people), does any of you have a pointer where to find either a 
> reasonably priced, programmable router or a Linux router package for a 
> DIY box? It should somehow work together with DHCP: if a guest pays for 
> Internet (s)he should receive an IP address and timed access to the 
> Internet.

NoCat was the first thing that came to mind.  It's what's known as a
"Captive Portal", redirecting users to a webpage until they authenticate.  
http://nocat.net/

A Freshmeat search for "nocat" turned up a couple of systems for doing
roughly what you want:
http://freshmeat.net/projects/icam/
http://freshmeat.net/projects/zonecd/

As an aside, charging for access is typically more trouble than it's
worth.  Like cable TV and other "free" stuff, it's all about drawing
customers from your competitors, even though your room rate's a couple
bucks higher.  It's just promotional overhead.

But hey, if they can make back the cost of the billing system before
the place down the block puts "Free Wi-Fi" on their sign, that's cool.

Waiting to see "Free VoIP long-distance" next,
  Aaron

From nickaubert at gmail.com  Sun Jul 31 12:45:45 2005
From: nickaubert at gmail.com (Nick Aubert)
Date: Sun, 31 Jul 2005 12:45:45 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Wireless PCMCIA cards
In-Reply-To: <1122665085.3385.106.camel@eel.myhealthbank.com>
References: <20050728233147.B20897@defiant.ncplus.net>
	<1122665085.3385.106.camel@eel.myhealthbank.com>
Message-ID: <55ef240d05073112454cb5c18c@mail.gmail.com>

I got a D-Link AirPlus PCMCIA card for my laptop.  This model
(DWL-G630) happens to be a rebranded Atheros, and uses the madwifi
drivers.  The madwifi drivers are developed and distributed by
Atheros.  They're proprietary.  Getting my card working with Gentoo
took a bit of effort, including recompiling the kernel with the proper
modules, etc.  Of course, Gentoo is about as far from plug-n-play as
you can get, so this isn't necessarily a strike against madwifi
itself.  Luckily it has pretty good documentation:

http://madwifi.sourceforge.net/dokuwiki/doku.php

I haven't been able to get the card working with WPA encryption, but
it's been a while since I've tried.  I'm currently relying on
"security through obscurity" :-/  I know that other people have gotten
madwifi working with WPA, I just haven't had the time to figure out
how.  Aside from that, the card performs well.

-Nick

From znmeb at cesmail.net  Sun Jul 31 15:01:51 2005

For a little more (say $700US) you can probably get a new low-end laptop 
that's about to be replaced with a more advanced technology. This time 
of year, the major electronics places have "Back to School" sales, so 
shop around. The advantage of getting, say, a year-old but new laptop, 
is that you'll get a decent-sized hard drive and the Linux community 
will have had a chance to come up with drivers for it.

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Sun Jul 31 21:14:01 2005
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Sun, 31 Jul 2005 21:14:01 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] ThinkPad is Stuck at Runlevel 5 Login
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0507311612470.4702@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0507311546500.4702@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<20050731230448.GC16432@patch.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.62.0507311612470.4702@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <20050801041401.GC11370@gate.kl-ic.com>


On Sun, 31 Jul 2005, Michael Rasmussen wrote:
>Use <CTRL><ALT><F1> (or F2, F3) and get a console session.  Then you can
>check  ~/.xsession-errors

On Sun, Jul 31, 2005 at 04:14:03PM -0700, Rich Shepard wrote:
>   I should have mentioned that was the first thing I tried; I get a blank
> screen, but no prompt. The laptop is running Slackware-10.1 and I just
> upgraded xfce from -4.2.0 to -4.2.1.

Something is deeply wrong.  You have a Thinkpad 600E, and those machines
are rather perverse to deal with if the hard disk won't boot, IIRC.  If
you can't get at a console with <CTRL><ALT><F1>, or stop X entirely with
<CTRL><ALT><BS>, then you will have to boot from a floppy (which is
external on the 600E) or else pull the hard drive and work on it on 
another machine (12 screws and a 2.5-to-3.5 adapter).  I suspect your
upgrade process failed or was interrupted at the wrong point, and it
damaged one of many critical files necessary to boot.

If it was a different kind of laptop, capable of booting from a CD
(600E's are not, I believe), then you could boot from a Knoppix disk
and fix things with that.

Keith

# from Keith Lofstrom
# on Sunday 31 July 2005 09:14 pm:

>If it was a different kind of laptop, capable of booting from a CD
>(600E's are not, I believe), then you could boot from a Knoppix disk
>and fix things with that.

The Smart Boot Manager (was sbm.bin on the debian CD ~ Potato (looks 
like you'll have to build a floppy from source with the link below)) 
has been really handy for booting CD's on older machines.  Just put it 
on a floppy and boot, then select the cdrom.  Not sure what happens 
with exotic hardware, but if it can see the drive, you're golden.

  http://btmgr.webframe.org/

That assumes (of course) that you can have a floppy and cdrom attached 
to the machine at the same time.
